The Classification of Infinite Series: Order and Existence
This passage classifies infinite series by order and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational basis for distinguishing between impossible and possible infinite series, based on the presence or absence of positional or natural order.

Core Idea
Only ordered infinite series are impossible; unordered ones can be infinite.

This passage from al-Shahrastani's Nihayat al-Aqdam presents a key principle of rational theology: not all infinite series are impossible. By analyzing the nature of order and existence, the author distinguishes between cases where infinity leads to contradiction and where it does not. We will explore this classification and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get an overall sense of the argument before diving into analysis.
Notice that the passage is structured as a rule with examples.
2
Identify the two agreed-upon impossibilities
The passage begins by stating a consensus: infinite causes/effects and infinite bodies are impossible. Understand what these are.
These are foundational assumptions shared by the philosophers.
3
Understand the controlling principle
The author gives a general rule: any set whose units exist together with positional order, or exist successively with natural order, cannot be infinite. Focus on the two kinds of order.
Positional order refers to spatial arrangement; natural order refers to causal sequence.
4
See the examples of impossible infinity
Examples: a body (positional order) and a chain of causes (natural order). These clarify the rule.
5
Study the exception: possible infinite series
The passage explains that when there is no positional or natural order, infinity is not impossible. Examples: human souls (simultaneous but no spatial order) and circular motions (successive but cyclical).
This is the key nuance: not all infinities are irrational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واتفقوا على استحالة وجود علل ومعلولات لا تتناهى، واتفقوا أيضا على استحالة وجود أجسام لا تنتهي بالفعل، والضابط لمذهبهم فيما يتناهى وما لا يتناهى أن كل عدد فرضت آحاده موجودة معا وله ترتيب وضعي، أو فرضت آحاده متعاقبة في الوجود وله ترتيب طبيعي، فإن وجود ما لا نهاية له فيه مستحيل. مثال القسم الأول: جسم أو بعد لا يتناهى. ومثال القسم الثاني : علل ومعلولات لا تتناهى وما عدى ذلك كل جملة وعدد فرضت آحاده معًا، أو متعاقبة لا ترتيب لها وضعا ولا طبعا، فإن وجود ما لا نهاية له فيه غير مستحيل. مثال القسم الأول : نفوس إنسانية لا تتناهى وهي معا في الوجود بعد مفارقة الأبدان. ومثال القسم الثاني : حركات دورية وهي متعاقبة في الوجود، ومدار المسألة على الفرق بين الإيجاد والإيجاب، وبين التقدم والتأخر، وأن ما حصره الوجود فهو متناه من غير فرق بين الأقسام، وما لا يتناهى قط لا يتصور إلا في
And they agreed on the impossibility of the existence of an infinite series of causes and effects, and they also agreed on the impossibility of the existence of bodies that are actually infinite. And the controlling principle (al-dabit) for their doctrine regarding what is finite and what is infinite is that every number whose units are assumed to exist together and has a positional order (tartib wad'i), or whose units are assumed to be successive in existence and has a natural order (tartib tabi'i), then the existence of what is infinite in it is impossible. Example of the first type: a body or an extension that is infinite. Example of the second type: an infinite series of causes and effects. And everything else, a totality and number whose units are assumed together or successive without having a positional or natural order, then the existence of what is infinite in it is not impossible. Example of the first type: infinite human souls existing together after separation from bodies. Example of the second type: circular motions (harakat dawriyya) which are successive in existence. And the crux of the matter revolves around the distinction between creation (ijad) and necessitation (ijab), and between priority and posteriority, and that whatever is encompassed by existence is finite without distinction between the types, and that which is infinite cannot be conceived except in [temporal extension?].
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents a logical classification of infinite series. The author first notes that all philosophers (presumably those mentioned earlier) agree on the impossibility of an actual infinite in two cases: (1) an infinite body or extension (simultaneous existence with spatial order), and (2) an infinite regress of causes and effects (successive with natural/causal order). Then he states the positive rule: any set whose members exist simultaneously and have positional order, or whose members exist successively and have natural order, cannot be infinite. Examples are given. Conversely, if the set lacks that order, infinity is possible. Examples: human souls existing simultaneously after death (no spatial order), and circular motions (successive but cyclical, not linear causal chain). He concludes by noting the key distinctions: between ijad (creation ex nihilo) and ijab (necessitation), and between priority and posteriority. The point is that whatever is encompassed by existence is finite; what is infinite cannot be conceived except perhaps in a non-ordinal sense.

Key Terms
علل ومعلولات
أجسام لا تتناهى
ترتيب وضعي
ترتيب طبيعي
الإيجاد
الإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All infinite series are logically impossible.
Correction: The passage shows that only infinite series with positional or natural order are impossible; unordered series like souls or circular motions can be infinite without contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of something that can be infinite without contradiction?
A) A chain of physical causes
B) An infinite body
C) Human souls after death
D) A series of numbers with positional order

Key Takeaway
The possibility of an infinite series depends on whether the series has a positional or natural order; without such order, infinity is not rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the afterlife and the endless existence of souls, does this lesson's distinction between ordered and unordered infinities give you a new intellectual grounding for that belief? How does that affect your hope and patience now?
?
Have you ever been troubled by the idea of an infinite causal chain, wondering if God's creation could have always existed? How does the distinction between ijad (free creation) and ijab (necessitation) reframe your trust in God's sovereignty and your personal gratitude for being created by will, not necessity?
?
Consider the cycles in your own life—habits, routines, recurring sins. Are they like 'circular motions' without order, or do you strive to give them purpose and direction? How does this passage call you to transform endless cycles into meaningful, ordered progression toward God?

When Infinity Is Impossible: The Rule of Order
The author states the rule for infinite chains with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between infinite quantities that are impossible (those with order) and those that are possible (without order), as established by classical theologians.

Core Idea
Infinity is impossible only when units are ordered — positional or natural.

This lesson explains a key rule in classical theology regarding infinity. The author presents a classification that determines when an infinite series is impossible and when it is not. This rule is essential for arguments about the eternity of the world and the nature of souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two agreed-upon impossibilities at the beginning, then the general rule, then the examples.
The passage is dense but structured; pay attention to the terms 'ترتيب وضعي' and 'ترتيب طبيعي'.
2
Identify the rule's two subcategories
The rule mentions two conditions that make infinite impossible: units existing simultaneously with positional order, and units existing successively with natural order. Write them down.
Positional order refers to spatial ordering (e.g., parts of a body); natural order refers to causal or sequential dependency.
3
Analyze the examples
For each subcategory, the author gives an example of impossible infinite and an example of possible infinite. List them and note why the possible ones lack order.
Human souls simultaneously existing have no positional order; circular motions successive have no natural order (no beginning or end).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢) واتفقوا على استحالة وجود علل ومعلولات لا تتناهى، واتفقوا أيضا على استحالة وجود أجسام لا تنتهي بالفعل، والضابط لمذهبهم فيما يتناهى وما لا يتناهى أن كل عدد فرضت آحاده موجودة معا وله ترتيب وضعي، أو فرضت آحاده متعاقبة في الوجود وله ترتيب طبيعي، فإن وجود ما لا نهاية له فيه مستحيل. مثال القسم الأول: جسم أو بعد لا يتناهى. ومثال القسم الثاني: علل ومعلولات لا تتناهى وما عدى ذلك كل جملة وعدد فرضت آحاده معًا، أو متعاقبة لا ترتيب لها وضعا ولا طبعا، فإن وجود ما لا نهاية له فيه غير مستحيل. مثال القسم الأول: نفوس إنسانية لا تتناهى وهي معا في الوجود بعد مفارقة الأبدان. ومثال القسم الثاني: حركات دورية وهي متعاقبة في الوجود.
(2) They agreed on the impossibility of the existence of causes and effects that are infinite, and they also agreed on the impossibility of the existence of bodies that do not end in actuality. The rule for their doctrine concerning what is finite and what is infinite is that every number whose units are assumed to exist simultaneously and have a positional order, or whose units are assumed to be successive in existence and have a natural order, then the existence of what is infinite in it is impossible. Example of the first category: a body or dimension that does not end. Example of the second category: causes and effects that are infinite. Other than that, every whole and number whose units are assumed to be simultaneous, or successive, having no order, positional or natural, then the existence of what is infinite in it is not impossible. Example of the first category: human souls that are infinite and exist simultaneously after separation from bodies. Example of the second category: circular motions, which are successive in existence.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents a classification and rule regarding the possibility of infinite quantities. The author states that there is agreement (ittifāq) on the impossibility of an infinite chain of causes and effects, and also on the impossibility of an infinite actual body. He then gives the rule (al-ḍābiṭ) for what can be infinite and what cannot: if units (āḥād) are either simultaneous with a positional order (tartīb waḍʿī) or successive with a natural order (tartīb ṭabīʿī), then an infinite is impossible. For example, an infinite body (simultaneous with positional order) and an infinite chain of causes (successive with natural order) are impossible. Conversely, if units are simultaneous but without order, or successive without order, then an infinite is not impossible. Examples: infinite human souls existing simultaneously after death (simultaneous without order) and circular motions (like the rotation of celestial spheres) that are successive in existence but without a natural order (since they have no beginning or end). This classification is foundational for later arguments about the eternity of the world and the possibility of infinite souls.

Key Terms
علل ومعلولات
ترتيب وضعي
ترتيب طبيعي
متناه
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that an infinite number of anything is logically impossible.
Correction: The passage clarifies that infinity is impossible only when there is a positional or natural order among the units. Unordered infinities, such as simultaneous souls or circular motions, are not impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following can be infinite without contradiction?
A) An infinite chain of causes and effects
B) An infinite body
C) Infinite human souls existing simultaneously
D) An infinite number with natural order

Key Takeaway
The rule distinguishes ordered infinities (impossible) from unordered infinities (possible).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a product of an infinite chain of causes? No, the rule says it must be finite. Does the fact that your existence depends ultimately on a First Cause, God, make you feel accountable to Him in every moment? How will you respond to that dependency today?
?
Your soul will exist forever after death, along with countless others, without spatial order. What kind of order are you cultivating in your soul now—faith and righteousness? Or chaos and negligence? What will you change today to ensure your soul is in the right state for eternity?
?
Circular motions can be infinite without natural order—like the cycles of your daily life. Are your routines just empty repetitions, or are they ordered towards God? What one cycle in your life can you infuse with intention and worship starting today?

The Four Types of Priority and Posteriority
The author enumerates the four types of priority and posteriority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of priority and posteriority and why they are essential for theological disputes.

Core Idea
Priority comes in four distinct senses: time, space, virtue, and causality.

Before entering the debate on the world's creation, we must first agree on what 'priority' means. The author classifies four traditional types of priority, which clarify the subject of dispute. This lesson presents those four types.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's emphasis on needing to clarify the subject of dispute before listing the types.
2
Identify each type of priority
The passage lists four types. For each type, note the name and the example given (e.g., temporal: father/son; spatial: imam/congregation).
The third type is also called priority by rank.
3
Reflect on the role of these categories
Ask yourself why these four categories are necessary for theological arguments. The commentary will help you see how they prevent confusion in disputes about creation and eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بد من بحث على محل النزاع حتى يكون التوارد بالنفي والإثبات على محل واحد من وجه واحد، فيصبح انقسام الصدق والكذب والحق والباطل ولا يتبين محل النزاع إلا بالبحث عن أقسام التقدم والتأخر.

فقال القوم: التقدم والتأخر يطلق ويراد به التقدم بالزمان كتقدم الوالد على الولد، ويطلق ويراد به التقدم بالمكان والتأخر به؛ كتقدم الإمام على المأموم، وقد يسمى هذا القسم متقدما بالرتبة، وقد يطلق ويراد به الفضيلة؛ كتقدم العالم على الجاهل، وقد يطلق ويراد به التقدم بالذات والتأخر بها؛ كتقدم العلة على المعلول، وهؤلاء قصدوا في إطلاق لفظ الذات، فإنهم أرادوا به العلية، والذات أعم من العلية فكان من حقهم أن يقولوا التقدم بالعلية، ثم العلية الغابية تتقدم على المعلول في الذهن والتصور لا في الوجود، فهي متأخرة في الوجود متقدمة في الذهن، بخلاف العلة الفاعلية والعلل الصورية فإنها لا تكون مقارنة في الوجود. فافهم هذا فإنه ينفعك في نفس المسألة؛ حيث يستشهدون بشعاع الشمس مع الشمس، وحركة الكم مع حركة اليد، فإنهما وإن تقارنا في الزمان أنهما إذا أخذا على أن أحدهما سبب والثاني مسبب لم يتقارنا في الوجود؛ لأن وجود أحدهما من وجود الآخر، فوجود المفيد كيف يقارن وجود المستفيد، لكن إذا أخذا مستفاد
It is necessary to investigate the subject of dispute so that the coming together of negation and affirmation is upon one locus from one aspect, and thus the division of truth and falsehood and right and wrong becomes sound. And the subject of dispute is not clarified except by investigating the types of priority and posteriority.

Then the people said: Priority and posteriority are used, and by them is meant temporal priority, like the priority of the father over the son; and they are used, and by them is meant spatial priority and posteriority by it, like the priority of the imam over the follower, and this category may be called priority by rank; and they are used, and by them is meant priority in virtue, like the priority of the scholar over the ignorant; and they are used, and by them is meant priority by essence (al-dhāt) and posteriority by it, like the priority of the cause over the effect. And these people intended by the term 'essence' causality, but essence is more general than causality, so it was right for them to say priority by causality. Then the final cause is prior to the effect in mind and conception, not in existence; it is posterior in existence and prior in the mind, unlike the efficient cause and the formal causes, for they are not concurrent in existence. Understand this, for it will benefit you in the very issue; where they cite as evidence the ray of sunlight with the sun, and the movement of the ring with the movement of the hand. For although they are concurrent in time, if they are taken such that one is a cause and the other an effect, they are not concurrent in existence, because the existence of one is from the existence of the other. So how can the existence of the benefactor be concurrent with the existence of the beneficiary? But if they are taken as derived...
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage begins with a methodological point: to resolve disputes about infinity and creation, one must first clarify the subject of dispute (maḥall al-nizāʿ) so that opposing claims apply to the same thing. This requires examining the types of priority (taqaddum) and posteriority (ta'akhkhur). The author then reports the classification given by 'the people' (al-qawm). Priority is used in four senses: (1) temporal priority, e.g., father before son; (2) spatial priority, e.g., imam before congregation, also called priority by rank; (3) priority in virtue, e.g., scholar before ignorant; (4) priority by essence (al-dhāt), e.g., cause before effect. However, the author notes that 'essence' is broader than causality, so they should have said 'priority by causality' (al-taʿlīl). He then distinguishes types of causes: the final cause (al-ʿilla al-ghāʾiyya) is prior in the mind but posterior in existence, whereas the efficient cause (al-ʿilla al-fāʿiliyya) and formal causes (al-ʿilal al-ṣūriyya) are not concurrent in existence with their effects. He gives examples: sunlight and the sun, and the movement of a ring (kamm) with the movement of the hand. Though concurrent in time, if one is cause and the other effect, they are not simultaneous in existence because the effect's existence derives from the cause. The passage ends abruptly with 'but if they are taken as derived...', suggesting further discussion. This classification is crucial for arguments about the relationship between God and the world.

Key Terms
تقدم
تأخر
الزمان
المكان
الفضيلة
الذات
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of priority is exemplified by the precedence of the imam over the congregation?
A) Temporal priority
B) Spatial priority
C) Priority in virtue
D) Priority by essence

Key Takeaway
The four types of priority are temporal, spatial, by virtue, and by essence (causality).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about why you believe in God, do you assume God must have existed before the world in time? How does distinguishing types of priority challenge or clarify your reasoning? Will you reevaluate how you present the Creator-creature relationship to others?
?
Have you ever mistaken your own intellectual priority (e.g., being right) for temporal priority, leading to arrogance? How can this simple classification help you humbly examine your assumptions in debates?
?
In what areas of your life do you confuse different kinds of priority—like giving precedence to your desires over your duties (virtue priority vs. time priority)? What concrete step can you take this week to align your priorities with what truly matters in essence?

Causal Priority and the Distinction of Final Cause
The author critiques the term 'essence' and explains causal priority with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority by essence should be called priority by causality, and how the final cause differs from efficient and formal causes.

Core Idea
Final causes are prior in mind but not in existence; efficient causes are not concurrent in existence with their effects.

After listing the four types, the author critiques calling the fourth type 'priority by essence' and clarifies that it is really causality. He then distinguishes between final causes, which are prior in the mind, and efficient/formal causes, which are not concurrent in existence with their effects. The lesson uses examples like sunlight and the sun to illustrate these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Notice that it begins by critiquing the term 'essence' and then introduces a key distinction among causes.
2
Understand the critique and the causal types
The author argues that 'essence' is broader than causality, so the fourth priority should be called 'priority by causality'. Then he distinguishes final causes (prior in mind) from efficient and formal causes (not concurrent in existence).
3
Analyze the examples
The examples of sunlight/sun and ring/hand show that even when two things are concurrent in time, if one is a cause and the other an effect, they are not concurrent in existence. The cause's existence is prior in essence.
The passage ends midsentence, but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بد من بحث على محل النزاع حتى يكون التوارد بالنفي والإثبات على محل واحد من وجه واحد، فيصبح انقسام الصدق والكذب والحق والباطل ولا يتبين محل النزاع إلا بالبحث عن أقسام التقدم والتأخر.

فقال القوم: التقدم والتأخر يطلق ويراد به التقدم بالزمان كتقدم الوالد على الولد، ويطلق ويراد به التقدم بالمكان والتأخر به؛ كتقدم الإمام على المأموم، وقد يسمى هذا القسم متقدما بالرتبة، وقد يطلق ويراد به الفضيلة؛ كتقدم العالم على الجاهل، وقد يطلق ويراد به التقدم بالذات والتأخر بها؛ كتقدم العلة على المعلول، وهؤلاء قصدوا في إطلاق لفظ الذات، فإنهم أرادوا به العلية، والذات أعم من العلية فكان من حقهم أن يقولوا التقدم بالعلية، ثم العلية الغابية تتقدم على المعلول في الذهن والتصور لا في الوجود، فهي متأخرة في الوجود متقدمة في الذهن، بخلاف العلة الفاعلية والعلل الصورية فإنها لا تكون مقارنة في الوجود. فافهم هذا فإنه ينفعك في نفس المسألة؛ حيث يستشهدون بشعاع الشمس مع الشمس، وحركة الكم مع حركة اليد، فإنهما وإن تقارنا في الزمان أنهما إذا أخذا على أن أحدهما سبب والثاني مسبب لم يتقارنا في الوجود؛ لأن وجود أحدهما من وجود الآخر، فوجود المفيد كيف يقارن وجود المستفيد، لكن إذا أخذا مستفاد
It is necessary to investigate the subject of dispute so that the coming together of negation and affirmation is upon one locus from one aspect, and thus the division of truth and falsehood and right and wrong becomes sound. And the subject of dispute is not clarified except by investigating the types of priority and posteriority.

Then the people said: Priority and posteriority are used, and by them is meant temporal priority, like the priority of the father over the son; and they are used, and by them is meant spatial priority and posteriority by it, like the priority of the imam over the follower, and this category may be called priority by rank; and they are used, and by them is meant priority in virtue, like the priority of the scholar over the ignorant; and they are used, and by them is meant priority by essence (al-dhāt) and posteriority by it, like the priority of the cause over the effect. And these people intended by the term 'essence' causality, but essence is more general than causality, so it was right for them to say priority by causality. Then the final cause is prior to the effect in mind and conception, not in existence; it is posterior in existence and prior in the mind, unlike the efficient cause and the formal causes, for they are not concurrent in existence. Understand this, for it will benefit you in the very issue; where they cite as evidence the ray of sunlight with the sun, and the movement of the ring with the movement of the hand. For although they are concurrent in time, if they are taken such that one is a cause and the other an effect, they are not concurrent in existence, because the existence of one is from the existence of the other. So how can the existence of the benefactor be concurrent with the existence of the beneficiary? But if they are taken as derived...
Source pages p. 7

Commentary

This requires examining the types of priority (taqaddum) and posteriority (ta'akhkhur). The author then reports the classification given by 'the people' (al-qawm). He gives examples: sunlight and the sun, and the movement of a ring (kamm) with the movement of the hand. The passage ends abruptly with 'but if they are taken as derived...', suggesting further discussion. This classification is crucial for arguments about the relationship between God and the world.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cause and effect always exist simultaneously in time.
Correction: They may be concurrent in time, but the effect's existence depends on the cause, so they are not concurrent in existence; the cause is prior in essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the final cause is prior in which sense?
A) Prior in existence only
B) Prior in the mind only
C) Prior both in existence and mind
D) Prior in neither

Key Takeaway
Final causes are prior in the mind but posterior in existence, while efficient causes are not concurrent in existence with their effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What purpose do you conceive daily but have not yet realized? Does that purpose actually guide your actions, or do you let efficient causes distract you?
?
When you see two events happening at the same time, do you sometimes doubt that one caused the other? How does this lesson's distinction between temporal simultaneity and causal priority challenge your reliance on Allah's causation?
?
Think of a recent decision. Were you more concerned with the 'how' (efficient cause) or the 'why' (final cause)? Which should have taken priority in your heart, and why does that matter for your spiritual state?

Cause and Effect: Existential Priority
The author illustrates existential priority using examples of sunlight and key movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal simultaneity does not imply existential simultaneity in causal relationships, and how referencing all existence to God resolves the apparent coexistence of cause and effect.

Core Idea
Cause and effect cannot be existentially simultaneous because one depends on the other.

The author uses vivid examples to clarify a subtle point about causality. Though sunlight and the sun appear together, the sun's existence is prior to the ray's. Similarly, in theology, understanding existential priority helps avoid confusion. This passage illustrates that real coexistence only occurs when both are directly from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the examples the author gives: sunlight and the sun, key movement and hand movement.
These examples are chosen because they are temporally simultaneous.
2
Identify the distinction
The author points out that even if two things occur at the same time, if one is the cause and the other the effect, they are not concurrent in existence. Ask yourself: why can't a cause and its effect coexist existentially?
Because the effect's existence comes from the cause, so the cause must be prior in existence.
3
Understand the resolution
When both the cause and effect are considered as receiving existence directly from the Bestower of forms (God), then they do coexist existentially, and the causal relationship between them is removed. This shows how God's direct creation changes the picture.
This connects to the larger theological point that all secondary causes are ultimately dependent on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فافهم هذا فإنه ينفعك في نفس المسألة؛ حيث يستشهدون بشعاع الشمس مع الشمس، وحركة الكم مع حركة اليد، فإنهما وإن تقارنا في الزمان أنهما إذا أخذا على أن أحدهما سبب والثاني مسبب لم يتقارنا في الوجود؛ لأن وجود أحدهما من وجود الآخر، فوجود المفيد كيف يقارن وجود المستفيد، لكن إذا أخذا على أن وجودهما مستفاد من وجود واهب الصور فحينئذ يتقارنان في الوجود، وهناك لا يكون أحدهما سببا والثاني مسببا.
Understand this, for it benefits you in the same issue: where they cite as evidence the ray of the sun with the sun, and the movement of the key with the movement of the hand. For these two, even if they are concurrent in time, when they are taken such that one is a cause and the other an effect, they are not concurrent in existence, because the existence of one comes from the existence of the other. So how can the existence of the giver (mufid) be concurrent with the existence of the recipient (mustafid)? But if they are taken such that their existence is derived from the existence of the Bestower of forms (wahib al-suwar), then they are concurrent in existence, and there neither is a cause nor an effect.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage provides a practical illustration of the distinction between temporal and existential priority. The author notes that scholars often adduce examples like sunlight and the sun, or the movement of a key and the movement of a hand. Though these pairs are simultaneous in time, if one is considered the cause and the other the effect, they cannot be considered concurrent in existence because the effect's existence depends on the cause. However, if both are considered as receiving existence directly from the Giver of forms (wahib al-suwar, i.e., God), then they are concurrent in existence, and the causal relationship between them is removed. This underscores the author's point about the distinction between existential and temporal copresence.

Key Terms
شعاع الشمس
حركة اليد
المفيد
المستفيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: If two things occur at the same time, they must be existentially simultaneous, and one can be the cause of the other without ontological priority.
Correction: The author shows that temporal simultaneity does not imply existential simultaneity. In a cause-effect pair, the cause must exist before the effect in the order of existence, even if they appear at the same time. True existential coexistence only occurs when both are directly from God.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Two things cannot existentially coexist if one is the cause of the other; only when both are directly created by God do they truly coexist without a causal relationship.

The Methodological Rule for Conceiving Causal Priority and Necessity
The author prescribes the method for conceiving causality: first assume only existential priority, then consider necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct methodological order for analyzing causal relationships: existential priority before necessity.

Core Idea
Existential priority precedes necessity in causal reasoning.

This passage from al-Ash'ari's work establishes a crucial methodological rule for kalam reasoning about causality. Before we can determine whether a cause necessitates its effect, we must first assume the mere existential priority of the cause over the effect. The author then shows how to properly order the concepts of existence and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a very precise, step-by-step instruction.
2
Identify the two stages
The passage presents two stages: first, assume existential priority of the giver over the recipient 'from the perspective of existence only'; second, after that, consider whether the recipient's existence is necessary by the giver.
The first stage deliberately excludes any notion of causation.
3
Understand the logical constraint
The author states the correct order of phrases: 'This came into existence from it, so it is necessary by it' is valid, but the reverse is not. This underscores that existence from a cause is the basis for necessity, not the other way around.
This guards against a common fallacy in causal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يجب أن يفرض تقدم وجود المفيد على وجود المستفيد من حيث الوجود فقط، من غير أن يخطر بالبال كون المفيد علة لذاته أو موجدا له بصفة، ثم يقع بعد ذلك التفات إلى أن الوجود المستفيد وجود واجب به؛ لأنه علته أو لا يجب به، وذلك لأن الوجوب بالغير لازم به فإنه يحسن أن يقال: هذا قد وجد عنه فوجب به، ولا يجوز أن يقال: وجب به فوجد عنه.
Then it is necessary to assume the precedence of the existence of the giver (mufid) over the existence of the recipient (mustafid) from the perspective of existence only, without it occurring to the mind that the giver is a cause by itself or a bringer into existence by an attribute. Then after that, attention is paid to whether the existence of the recipient is an existence necessary by it, because it is its cause, or not necessary by it. And that is because necessity by another is necessarily consequent upon it; for it is good to say: This came into existence from it, so it is necessary by it; but it is not permissible to say: It is necessary by it, so it came into existence from it.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage provides a methodological rule for analyzing causal sequences. The author insists that when we consider a giver (mufid) and a recipient (mustafid), we must first assume the existential priority of the giver purely in terms of existence, without any preconception that the giver is a cause by essence or by an attribute. Only after establishing this existential priority do we then investigate whether the recipient's existence is necessary by the giver (i.e., if the giver is truly its cause) or not. The logical order is: existence from the giver, then necessity by the giver. The reverse—necessity first, then existence—is not permissible. This guards against a common fallacy in causal reasoning.

Key Terms
الوجود فقط
الوجوب بالغير
وجب به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is necessary by something else, then it must exist from it (necessity implies existence).
Correction: The author clarifies that existence from the cause is prior; necessity by the cause is consequent. The correct logical order is existence first, then necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is the correct logical order concerning the giver and the recipient?
A) The recipient is necessary by the giver, so it exists from it
B) The recipient exists from the giver, then is necessary by it
C) The giver is necessary by the recipient, so it exists
D) Existence and necessity are simultaneous without order

Key Takeaway
In causal reasoning, always begin with existential priority, then determine necessity—never the reverse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your relationship with Allah, do you ever assume His necessity as your cause before you have fully acknowledged His existence as prior? This lesson demands that you first affirm His existential priority—pure existence—before any thought of His being the cause of your being. Can you do that? Or do you mix the steps, thereby reversing the order that the author warns against?
?
In your daily life, how often do you say, 'This happened because of that,' without first checking whether 'that' even had existential priority? The lesson teaches a method: first assume priority, then check necessity. Where in your life have you reversed this order, and what confusion has it caused?
?
The author says we must 'assume the precedence of existence only' before considering causality. This requires intellectual discipline. Are you willing to train your mind to pause before attributing causes? Can you, when facing an event, first affirm 'this exists from Allah' without immediately attaching 'because He caused it'? Reflect on the purity of tawhid this demands.

The Principle of Concomitance (Ma'iyya) for Precedence and Posteriority
The author explains the principle of concomitance for each meaning of precedence and posteriority, and its inapplicability to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of concomitance applies to different types of precedence and posteriority, and why it cannot be applied to Allah.

Core Idea
Every precedence relation has a corresponding concomitance, but none applies to Allah.

The author presents a general rule: each form of precedence or posteriority has a specific kind of concomitance in its own rank, but that concomitance does not coexist with the precedence in that same rank. He illustrates with the cause-effect relationship and then makes a firm theological boundary: no form of concomitance can be attributed to Allah and the world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule
Read the first part of the passage which states that for each meaning of precedence and posteriority, there is a concomitance in its rank that does not coexist with the precedence/posteriority in that same rank but coexists in another rank.
This is a logical principle: precedence and concomitance are relative to ranks.
2
Examine the example
Look at the example of cause and effect: the cause precedes the effect in essence and existence, but they are concomitant in time or place. This clarifies how the rule works in a concrete case.
3
Grasp the theological implication
The passage concludes that no form of concomitance applies to Allah and the world. This is a key point in Islamic theology to preserve God's transcendence.
This prohibits any notion of God being in time, space, or existence alongside creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكل معنى من معاني التقدم والتأخر معية في مرتبته لا يجامع التقدم والتأخر في تلك المرتبة، ويجامع في مرتبة أخرى؛ مثاله: تقدم السبب على المسبب ذاتا ووجودًا أو مقارنته معا وزمانًا أو مكانًا، ولكن لا يجوز أن تطلق معية ما على الباري تعالى والعالم.
And for every meaning among the meanings of precedence and posteriority there is a concomitance (ma'iyya) in its rank that does not coexist with precedence and posteriority in that same rank, but coexists in another rank. An example: the precedence of the cause over the effect in essence and existence, or their concomitance together in time or place. But it is not permissible to apply any concomitance whatsoever to the Exalted Creator and the world.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents a general rule: each type of precedence or posteriority has a corresponding form of concomitance (ma'iyya, i.e., being together) at its own rank, but that form of concomitance does not coexist with the same precedence/posteriority in that same rank; rather, it coexists in a different rank. For example, a cause is prior to its effect in essence and existence, but they can be concomitant in time or place. The crucial theological point comes at the end: no form of concomitance (whether temporal, spatial, or existential) can be applied to the relationship between God (al-Bari) and the world. This strongly guards against any notion of God being in any way together with creation in a manner that would imply limitation or association.

Key Terms
معية
مرتبة
الباري تعالى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since Allah is the cause of the world, He and the world are concomitant in some respect (e.g., time or space).
Correction: The author explicitly prohibits applying any concomitance to Allah and the world, preserving divine transcendence.

Checkpoint

B) No, it is not permissible

D) Only existential concomitance

Key Takeaway
No form of concomitance between Allah and the world is permissible; this safeguards divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The General Method of Proving the World's Temporality
The author states the two aims and details the first: proving huduth by accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four-step method used by theologians to prove that the world is temporally originated (huduth) and why this method refutes the idea of pre-eternity.

Core Idea
The world's temporality is proven through four inseparable steps about accidents and substances.

In this passage, the author outlines the foundational method that Muslim theologians have used to demonstrate that the world is not eternal but temporally originated. This method proceeds through four clear principles, starting from the existence of accidents and culminating in the impossibility of an infinite past. Understanding this method is crucial for grasping the later proofs for God's existence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main aims
The passage begins by distinguishing two aims: proving the world's occurrence and invalidating pre-eternity. Notice the structure.
The second aim is mentioned but not elaborated here.
2
Trace the four-step proof
List the four principles: (a) establishing accidents, (b) proving their temporality, (c) impossibility of substances being free from accidents, (d) impossibility of infinite temporal events. See how each step builds on the previous.
These steps are the standard theological method for proving huduth.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion: whatever is not preceded by temporal events is itself temporal. Ask why this proves the world is originated.
The conclusion ties directly to the first aim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما: إثبات حدث العالم. والثاني: إبطال القول بالقدم. أما الأول فقد سلك عامتهم طريق الإثبات بإثبات الأعراض أولا، وإثبات حدثها ثانيا، وبيان استحالة خلو الجواهر عنها ثالثا، وبيان استحالة حوادث لا أول لها رابعا، ويترتب على هذه الأصول أن ما لا يسبقه الحوادث فهو حادث. وقد أوردوا هذه الطريقة في كتبهم أحسن إيراد.
One of them: proving the occurrence of the world. And the second: invalidating the assertion of pre-eternity. As for the first, their majority followed a path of proof by establishing accidents first, and proving their occurrence second, and demonstrating the impossibility of substances being free from them third, and demonstrating the impossibility of temporal events with no beginning fourth, and it follows from these principles that what is not preceded by temporal events is itself temporal. And they have presented this method in their books in the best presentation.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the two main aims of the proof for the existence of God: first, proving that the world is originated (huduth), and second, refuting the doctrine of pre-eternity (qidam). The author then explains the first aim by outlining the standard method of the theologians: (1) establishing that accidents (a'rad) exist in bodies, (2) proving that accidents are temporal (haditha), (3) showing that substances (jawhar) cannot be free from accidents, and (4) demonstrating that it is impossible for there to be a series of temporal events without a beginning. From these principles, it follows that whatever is not preceded by such a series must itself be temporal. This method is foundational for the subsequent proofs.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
القدم
الأعراض
الجواهر
حوادث لا أول لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'what is not preceded by temporal events' refers to God, implying that God is temporal.
Correction: The phrase actually refers to the world itself: the world is not preceded by temporal events because it is the first temporal thing; thus it is temporally originated, not eternally existent.

Checkpoint

A) The existence of accidents
B) The temporality of accidents
C) The impossibility of substances being free from accidents
D) The impossibility of an infinite regress of temporal events

Key Takeaway
The method of proving the world's temporality rests on four principles about accidents and substances, leading to the conclusion that the world is originated.

?

?

Will you commit today to breaking one pattern, knowing that change is possible because everything temporal can be altered?
?
This passage is a method of proof. But do you rely more on such rational arguments or on the Quranic revelation for your certainty about creation? If you lean solely on one, what might that reveal about your faith? Pledge to study both reason and revelation equally as gifts from the Creator.

Al-Ash'ari's Refutation of Pre-Eternal Substances
Al-Ash'ari refutes pre-eternity of substances through change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's logical argument against the pre-eternity of substances and how change necessitates a cause.

Core Idea
Change in substances proves they cannot be eternal.

Al-Ash'ari provides a logical refutation of the claim that substances (jawhar) are pre-eternal. He argues that substances constantly change states of combination and separation, yet their essences cannot cause such changes. This leads to the necessity of an external cause and the conclusion that substances are temporally originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the alternatives mentioned
Notice the exhaustive list of possible states for substances: combined, separate, neither, both, etc. This shows the argument is comprehensive.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
Understand why the change in states requires a 'composer-separator' and how this leads to the temporality of substances.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the final principle: 'what is not preceded by a temporal event is itself temporal.' How does this seal the argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني فقد سلك شيخنا أبو الحسن الأشعري رضي الله عنه طريق الإبطال وقال: لو قدرنا قدم الجواهر لم يخل من أحد أمرين: إما أن تكون مجتمعة أو مفترقة، أو لا مجتمعة ولا مفترقة، أو مجتمعة ومفترقة معا، أو بعضها مجتمع وبعضها مفترق، وبالجملة ليست تخلو عن اجتماع وافتراق، أو جواز طريان الاجتماع والافتراق وتبدل أحدهما بالثاني، وهي بذواتها لا تجتمع ولا تفترق؛ لأن حكم الذات لا يتبدل وقد تبدلت، فلا بد من جامع فارق، ويترتب على هذه الأصول أن ما لا يسبقه حادث فهو حادث.
As for the second, our teacher Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may God be pleased with him, followed a path of refutation and said: If we suppose the pre-eternity of substances, they would not be free from one of two matters: either they are together or separate, or neither together nor separate, or together and separate simultaneously, or some together and some separate, and in summary they are not free from composition and separation, or the possibility of composition and separation occurring and one changing into the other, yet they by their essences do not compose or separate; because the ruling of the essence does not change, and they have changed, so there must be a composer-separator. And it follows from these principles that what is not preceded by a temporal event is itself temporal.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ash'ari's specific method for refuting the claim that substances (jawhar) are pre-eternal. He argues that if substances were eternal, they would necessarily be in some state of composition or division, or capable of such states. However, since the essence of a substance does not change by itself, yet we observe that substances do change from one state to another, there must be an external cause—a 'composer and separator' (jami' fariq). The conclusion is that anything not preceded by a temporal event (i.e., the change) is itself temporal, which disproves the pre-eternity of substances.

Key Terms
قدم الجواهر
اجتماع
افتراق
جامع فارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's argument is a direct proof for God's existence.
Correction: This argument specifically refutes the pre-eternity of matter, which is a step in the broader proof for the world's temporality and the existence of a Creator, but it is not itself a direct proof of God.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari conclude from the fact that substances change their states of composition and separation?
A) Substances are eternal because change is accidental
B) Substances require a composer-separator
C) Substances are self-sufficient
D) Substances are composed of atoms

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari shows that because substances change and cannot change themselves, they require an external cause, proving they are not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your own bad habits as if they are eternal—unchangeable? Al-Ash'ari proves that all substances are temporal and subject to change. What external cause are you relying on to transform your soul? Are you turning to the Composer-Separator, or are you expecting change from your own essence? Commit today to seek Him for the change you need.
?
You know that your heart changes—sometimes hard, sometimes soft. But you cannot change it by yourself. This lesson shows that the essence does not change itself. When was the last time you sincerely asked God to change your heart? If you haven't, you are acting as if your heart is eternal and unchangeable. Will you now ask the One who causes change?
?
Al-Ash'ari proves that change points to a cause. Look at your daily spiritual ups and downs. Do you attribute these changes to your own efforts or to God? If you believe you can engineer your own faith without dependence on God, you are making the same error as assuming substances change themselves. Repent from this independence and rely on the Creator of change.

Al-Isfarayini's Combined Method: Proof from Human Generation
Al-Isfarayini reformulates al-Ash'ari's method and adds a proof from human generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini reformulated al-Ash'ari's argument for God's existence, using the temporality and development of the human being as a key proof, and how this method applies universally to all bodies.

Core Idea
The changing human being points to an unchanging Creator.

This passage introduces the contribution of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who restated al-Ash'ari's cosmological argument in fresh terms. He particularly emphasizes the example of the human being, formed from a mixed drop and passing through stages of development. The argument is that the constant change in our essence and attributes cannot be caused by ourselves, our parents, or nature, necessitating an eternal, powerful, knowing Creator. Moreover, this reasoning extends from one human to all bodies by virtue of their shared corporeality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Isfarayini 'clothes' the method in a new expression.
2
Understand the human generation argument
Focus on the description of the human being's formation from a mixed drop and transformation through stages. Ask: why does this prove temporality?
The changes show that the human is not eternal.
3
Identify the conclusion
Find the sentence that states the human's need for a Creator. What attributes are mentioned?
The Creator is described as 'qadīm, qādir, 'alīm'.
4
Grasp the generalization
The passage says whatever holds for one person/body holds for all in terms of corporeality. Why does this matter?
This extends the proof from humans to all physical bodies.
5
Reflect on the combined method
Consider how this method both proves creation (temporality) and refutes pre-eternity.
It is a comprehensive argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أخذ الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني هذه الطريقة وكساها عبارة أخرى، وربما سلك أبو الحسن رحمه الله طريقا في إثبات حدوث الإنسان وتكونه من نطفة أمشاج وتقلبه في أطوار الخلقة وأكوار الفطرة، ولسنا نشك في أنه ما غير ذاته ولا بدل صفاته ولا الأبوان ولا الطبيعة فيتعين احتياجه إلى صانع قديم قادر علیم، قال: وما ثبت من الأحكام لشخص واحد أو الجسم واحد ثبت في الكل في الجسمية، وهذه الطريقة تجمع الإثبات والإبطال.
And the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini took this method and clothed it in another expression, and perhaps Abu al-Hasan, may God have mercy on him, followed a path in proving the temporality of man and his formation from a drop of mixed fluids and his transformation in the stages of creation and the cycles of nature, and we do not doubt that he did not change his essence nor alter his attributes nor the parents nor nature, so his need for an eternal, powerful, knowing Maker is determined. He said: Whatever rulings are established for a single person or a single body are established for all in terms of corporeality. And this method combines proof and refutation.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents the contribution of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who restated al-Ash'ari's method using different wording. It also mentions that al-Ash'ari himself sometimes took a different approach, proving the temporality of the human being by examining his origin from a mixed drop (nutfah amshāj) and his development through various stages. The argument is that since the human being's essence and attributes change, and neither parents nor nature cause this, the human being must depend on an eternal, powerful, knowing Creator. The key point is that whatever holds for one human or one body holds for all bodies by virtue of their shared corporeality. Thus this method simultaneously proves creation and refutes pre-eternity.

Correction: The argument uses the human being as a prime example, but the principle of corporeality extends it to all physical bodies.

Al-Isfarayini's argument demonstrates that the observable change in human beings necessitates an eternal Creator, and this reasoning applies universally to all bodies.

This argument denies that your parents or your own efforts are the real cause of your existence and transformation. ?
If this proof applies to all bodies, then every person you meet, every creature you see, is a sign of the same eternal Maker. How would your interactions with others change if you saw each person not as a product of biology or circumstance, but as a direct act of the ever-creating God?

Al-Juwayni's Specification Argument
Al-Juwayni presents his specification argument for a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Juwayni's argument that the world requires a specificator (mukhassis) because its contingent properties remain equally possible but only one configuration exists.

Core Idea
The world's particular configuration cannot be self-explanatory—it demands a specificator.

Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni presents an elegant argument that avoids reliance on the world's temporal origination. He points to the observable fact that many physical configurations are equally possible, yet only one is actual. This discrepancy, he argues, rationally necessitates a specificator—One who chooses among equally possible states. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and the translation to familiarize yourself with the flow and key terms.
2
Identify the core premise
Notice that al-Juwayni starts with the physical world's composition and states that alternative configurations are not impossible. This establishes the equal possibility of all states.
3
Follow the conclusion
Since all states are equally possible but only one is actual, reason demands a specificator. Also note his remarks about finitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتمد إمام الحرمين رضي الله عنه طريقة أخرى فقال: الأرض عند خصومنا محفوفة بالماء، والماء بالهواء، والهواء بالنار، والنار بالأفلاك وهي أجرام متحيزة شاغلة جوا وحيزا، وبالاضطرار نعلم أن فرض هذه الأجسام متيامنة عن مقرها أو متياسرة أو أكبر مما وجدت شكلا وعظما أو أصغر من ذلك ليس من المستحيلات، وكل مختص بوجه من وجوه الجواز دون سائر الوجوه، مع استواء الجائزات وتماثل الممكنات احتاج إلى مخصص بضرورة العقل، وستعلم فيما بعد أن العالم ممكن الوجود باعتبار ذاته؛ سواء قدر كونه متناهيا في ذاته مكانا أو زمانا أو غير متناه، فإن الخصم يقضي عليه بالإمكان على أنه في ذاته غير متناهي الزمان وهو متناهي المكان، فالأولى أن يجعل للتناهي المكاني مسألة، ويجعل للتناهي الزماني من حيث الحركات والمتحركات مسألة أخرى، ونأخذ احتياجه إلى المخصص كالمسلم أو كالضروري أو كالقريب من الضروري.
And the Imam of the Two Sanctuaries, may God be pleased with him, adopted another method and said: The earth, according to our opponents, is surrounded by water, water by air, air by fire, and fire by the celestial spheres—they are bodies occupying space, filling space and place. And we know necessarily that supposing these bodies to be to the right of their place or to the left, or larger than they are in shape and size, or smaller than that, is not among the impossibilities. And each is particularized by one aspect of the possible aspects to the exclusion of other aspects, while the possible things are equal and the contingent things are similar—so it requires a specificator by necessity of reason. And you will learn later that the world is possible in existence by virtue of its essence, whether it be finite in its essence in place or time or infinite; for the opponent judges it to be possible, considering it infinite in time but finite in place. So it is better to make the finitude of place one issue and to make the finitude of time in terms of motions and moving things another issue, and we take its need for a specificator as something accepted, or as necessary, or as close to necessary.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents the method of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), which is a specification argument. He begins by describing the physical structure of the world as understood at his time: earth, water, air, fire, and celestial spheres. He argues that it is not impossible for these bodies to be positioned differently (e.g., to the right or left) or to have different sizes. Since all these possibilities are equally possible, but only one configuration actually occurs, there must be a specificator (mukhassis) that selects this configuration. The necessity of such a specificator is known by reason. Al-Juwayni then notes that the world's possibility can be considered both in terms of spatial finitude and temporal finitude (motions), each being a distinct argument. He concludes that the need for a specificator is almost self-evident.

Key Terms
مخصص
جائزات
ممكنات
تناهي المكان
تناهي الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The specification argument only works if the world is finite in time or space.
Correction: Al-Juwayni explicitly states that the argument applies even if the world is infinite in time; the need for a specificator is independent of finitude.

Checkpoint
What does al-Juwayni argue based on the equal possibility of different configurations of bodies?
A) The world is eternal
B) The world requires a specificator
C) The world is composed of atoms
D) The world is an illusion

Key Takeaway
Al-Juwayni's specification argument shows that the actual configuration of the world, out of many equally possible ones, requires a specificator, and this need is self-evident to reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the specific layout of your own life—your circumstances, abilities, and challenges—do you see them as one possibility among many? If so, how does that realization press you to acknowledge the Specificator who chose this particular configuration for you?
?
Al-Juwayni says the need for a specificator is 'close to necessary' for reason. Ask yourself: Is this truth merely an intellectual conclusion, or does it stir your heart to gratitude and surrender? If it remains only in your mind, what barrier stands between you and living out this conviction?
?
Consider one 'small' thing in your day that could have been different—a conversation, a missed opportunity, a moment of clarity. Instead of dismissing it as chance, can you trace it back to the Hand that specified it? What would change in your prayer and trust if you did this consistently?

First Objection: How Is the Whole World Contingent?
Objection and answer on contingency of the whole world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the parts-to-whole argument to establish the contingency of the entire world.

Core Idea
The whole world is contingent because every part is contingent.

In this passage, an objector challenges the claim that the entire world is contingent. The author responds with a clear rational proof: sound reason judges each part of the world as possible in itself, and since the whole is composed of these parts, contingency necessarily applies to the whole. This argument is fundamental to grounding the need for a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a question followed by a concise answer.
The answer is a single sentence with a logical structure.
2
Analyze the logical argument
Identify the two premises: (1) Sound reason judges each part as possible. (2) The whole is composed of parts. Then see the conclusion: therefore the whole is necessarily possible.
This is a deductive argument from parts to whole.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument is important: it shows that the entire world is contingent and thus requires a cause beyond itself.
This connects to the broader proof for a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فما الدليل على تطرق وجود الجائزات إلى العالم بأسره؟ قلنا: العقل الصريح يقضي بالإمكان في كل واحد من أجزاء العالم، والمجموع إذا كان مركبا من الأجزاء كان الإمكان واجبا له ضرورة.
If it is said: What is the evidence for the entry of possibility into the entire world? We reply: Sound reason judges the possibility in each one of the parts of the world, and the whole, if it is composed of parts, the possibility is necessary for it necessarily.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector asks: How do we know that the whole world is subject to possibility (i.e., contingent)? The answer is that sound reason (al-'aql al-sarih) judges that every individual part of the world is possible in itself. Since the whole is composed of these parts, the whole must also be possible—indeed, necessarily possible. This establishes that the entire world is contingent and thus requires a cause.

Key Terms
العقل الصريح
الإمكان
أجزاء العالم
المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the whole could be necessary even if all its parts are contingent.
Correction: The author asserts that if the whole is composed of parts that are each contingent, then the whole must also be contingent. Composition does not cancel contingency.

Checkpoint

A) By analogy with other worlds
B) By scripture
C) By reasoning that each part is contingent and the whole is composed of them
D) By observing change in the world

Key Takeaway
The contingency of the whole world is proven by the contingency of its parts, showing the entire world depends on a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If every part of your life is contingent—from your health to your wealth—why do you often live as if you are in control? How does this lesson challenge that illusion?

Sound reason judges each part as possible—meaning it could cease to exist. When you look at your most cherished possessions or relationships, do you treat them as if they could vanish any moment? How can you train yourself to see God's hand in every contingent detail, and what action will you take today to cultivate that mindset?

Second objection: Proving bodies are finite
Objection and beginning of answer using thought experiment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial steps of the proof that bodies are finite, including the objection and the thought experiment that begins to derive a contradiction from assuming an infinite body.

Core Idea
Infinite bodies lead to logical contradictions in a thought experiment.

This passage presents a key objection in the debate over whether physical bodies are finite in size. The objector demands a proof, and the author begins to respond by inviting us to imagine an infinite body. The reasoning, though incomplete here, sets up a contradiction that shows why bodies must be finite. By examining this opening move, you'll see how kalam scholars use hypothetical reasoning to defend foundational beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the first answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the question-answer structure: 'If it is said... We reply...' The answer begins with a supposition.
The answer is cut off; we see only the start of the argument.
2
Analyze the supposition
The answer asks us to suppose a body without end, or a dimension without end. Consider what 'without end' means in terms of size. Then note the distinction: infinite in all directions or only one.
This distinction is important for the upcoming contradiction.
3
Identify the thought experiment's first move
Within the supposed infinite body, we can posit a finite point connected to an infinite line, and then another point. The argument is building toward a contradiction, though we don't see it here.
The incompleteness is due to the page break; the next part would continue the reasoning.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice that the proof does not rely on empirical observation but on rational deduction from a hypothetical scenario. This is typical of kalam arguments.
This approach is called 'proof by contradiction' or 'reductio ad absurdum'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فما الدليل على أن الأجسام متناهية من حيث الذات؟ قلنا: لو قدرنا جسما لا يتناهى، أو بعدا لا يتناهى، فإما أن يكون ذلك الجسم غير متناه من جميع الجهات أو من جهة واحدة، وعلى أي وجه قدر فيمكن أن يفرض فيه نقطة متناهية يتصل بها خط لا يتناهى ويمكن أن تفرض نقطة أخرى
If it is said: What is the evidence that bodies are finite in terms of essence? We reply: If we suppose a body without end, or a dimension without end, then either that body is infinite from all directions or from one direction, and in whatever way it is supposed, it is possible to posit in it a finite point connected to a line without end, and it is possible to posit another point
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage begins a second objection and answer concerning the finitude of bodies. The objector asks for proof that bodies are finite in themselves (i.e., have limited size). The answer starts a thought experiment: suppose an infinite body or infinite distance. The author then considers whether the infinite body is infinite in all directions or only one, and suggests that within such a body one could posit a finite point connected to an infinite line, and then another point, leading to a contradiction (though the argument is cut off). The passage is incomplete due to the page break.

Key Terms

لا يتناهى
بعد لا يتناهى
نقطة متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes what it tries to prove (that bodies are finite).
Correction: The argument does not assume finitude; it assumes an infinite body and derives a contradiction, showing that the assumption is false. So it's a proof by contradiction, not circular.

Checkpoint

A) The world is finite because God created it
B) We suppose a body without end
C) We suppose bodies are made of atoms
D) We suppose time is infinite

Key Takeaway
The proof that bodies are finite begins by assuming an infinite body and showing that this leads to a logical impossibility.

What is one assumption you hold that you have never subjected to the same logical scrutiny that the author applies here?
?
This argument begins by assuming the opposite (an infinite body) and then shows it leads to a contradiction. What is an assumption in your own life—perhaps about your own self-sufficiency or the permanence of this world—that, if examined closely, might lead to a contradiction with the truths of Islam? Proof of Contingency from Change and Composition
Formal proof for the world's contingency and need for a preponderant cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the formal kalam proof for the contingency of the world based on change, composition, and the need for a preponderant cause.

Core Idea
Everything that changes or is composed needs a cause outside itself.

In this passage, the author presents a logical demonstration (burhān) for the contingency of the world. He first argues that anything subject to change or composed of parts is contingent, meaning it could not exist on its own. Then he shows that the world as a whole, being composed of contingent parts, is contingent. Finally, he introduces the concept of a 'preponderant' (murājiḥ) that tips the scale from non-existence to existence, setting up the proof for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The passage begins with two conditional statements. Notice that each premise is based on observable features: change and multiplicity.
2
Understand the first proof from change
'Everything changeable or multiple is possible of existence.' Why? Because it could be otherwise.
3
Understand the second proof from composition
The whole is composed of parts; if each part is contingent, the whole must be contingent.
4
See the conclusion about the preponderant cause
Once contingency is established, existence needs a cause that tips the balance. Note that this cause cannot be the thing itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فوجه تركيب البرهان منه أن نقول : كل متغير أو متكثر فهو ممكن الوجود باعتبار ذاته، وكل ممكن الوجود باعتبار ذاته فوجوده بإيجاد غيره، فكل متغير أو متكثر فوجوده بإيجاد غيره، وأيضا فإن الكل مركب من الآحاد، والآحاد إذا كان كل واحد منها ممكن الوجود فالكل واجب أن يكون ممكن الوجود، وكل ممكن فهو باعتبار ذاته جايز أن يوجد وجايز أن لا يوجد ، وإذا ترجح جانب الوجود على العدم احتاج إلى مرجح والمرجح يستحيل أن يكون مرجحا باعتبار ذاته، ومن حيث وجوده فقط لأمور:
The way to compose the proof from it is to say: Everything changeable or multiple is possible of existence in itself, and everything possible of existence in itself has its existence by the bringing-into-existence of another. So everything changeable or multiple has its existence by the bringing-into-existence of another. Also, the whole is composed of parts, and if each part is possible of existence, then the whole must be possible of existence. And every possible is in itself contingent: it may exist and may not exist. When the side of existence outweighs non-existence, it needs a preponderant (murājiḥ). And the preponderant is impossible to be a preponderant by virtue of its essence and by virtue of its existence alone, for reasons:
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a formal kalam proof (burhān) for the contingency of the world. It first states the proof from change and multiplicity: anything subject to change or composed of parts is contingent in essence; a contingent being requires an external cause for its existence. Therefore, the world (changeable and composite) is contingent and needs a cause. The author then adds a second proof from composition: if each part of the world is contingent, the whole is also contingent. Once contingency is established, the existence of the world requires a preponderant (murājiḥ) that tips the scale from non-existence to existence. This preponderant cannot be the essence or existence of the world itself, because then it would be self-sufficient. The author then signals that he will give several reasons (umūr) why this is impossible, which are elaborated in the next lesson.

Key Terms
ممكن الوجود
إيجاد
مرجح
جواز
ترجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular by assuming what it wants to prove.
Correction: It does not assume contingency; it deduces it from change and composition, which are observable.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof for the world's contingency given in this passage?
A) The world is finite, so it cannot be necessary.
B) The world is composed of parts, each contingent, so the whole is contingent.
C) The world changes, so it must have a creator.
D) The world is many, so it cannot be a single necessary being.

Key Takeaway
The world's reliance on a cause is established through its changeability and composition, leading to the necessary need for a preponderant cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your own abilities and decisions every day. But this lesson proves that you, as a changing and composite being, cannot be the cause of your own existence. Right now, think of one area of your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient—your career, your relationships, your health. How does acknowledging your complete contingency on a preponderant cause change how you approach that area today? Commit to one action that reflects your dependence on the Necessary Being.
?
The proof shows that a preponderant cannot come from the thing itself. Consider your own achievements: you did not create your own intelligence, health, or opportunities. Yet we often take credit for success. Where in your heart do you feel pride as if you are the ultimate cause? Imagine stripping away that pride and attributing every good in your existence entirely to the Preponderant. How would that reshape your gratitude and humility right now? Write down one specific blessing you will acknowledge as purely from Him.
?
You live in a universe of change—your body, thoughts, circumstances all shift. If every changing thing needs a cause, and the preponderant cannot be the thing itself, then where is your ultimate anchor? This lesson confronts you with the fact that you are not a self-standing entity. In what ways do you deceive yourself into thinking you are stable or independent? What would it look like to live today fully aware that your very existence is being maintained moment by moment by the Preponderant? Describe a concrete habit you can adopt to remind yourself of this dependence.
