First Argument: The Commonality of Existence
The first argument: existence as a common attribute cannot explain why some things exist and others not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the common meaning of existence cannot explain the specification of creation, and why a qualifying attribute is necessary.

Core Idea
Existence is neutral; it cannot pick favorites.

Sanusi opens his refutation of intrinsic necessitation by pointing out that existence is a shared property between the Necessary and the contingent. If God caused by virtue of existence alone, all existents would be equally produced, which is contrary to observation. This forces the conclusion that a specifying quality or will must be involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read the Arabic passage for the first argument and its translation. Notice how Sanusi starts with a general premise about the nature of existence.
The phrase 'بمعنى واحد لا يختلف' is key.
2
Understand the implication
If existence is the same in both necessary and contingent, then acting by existence alone would produce all existents equally. Since they are not equally produced, there must be a qualifying attribute.
The conclusion is that He creates 'ذاتا على صفة' (essence qualified by a quality).
3
Connect to the refutation
This shows that the intrinsic necessitation (acting by mere essence) is false, because specification requires an additional factor beyond essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أن الوجود من حيث هو وجود أمر يعم الواجب والجايز، وهو فيهما بمعنى واحد لا يختلف ، فلو أوجد من حيث إنه وجود أو من حيث إنه ذات لما كان أحد الموجودين أولى بالإيجاد من الثاني، فيتعين أنه يوجد لكونه وجودًا على صفة أو ذاتا على صفة. ويدل على ذلك الأمر الثاني : وهو أن وجوه الجواز قد تطرقت إلى الأفعال، وتلك الوجوه متماثلة، والموجب لا يخصص مثلا عن مثل، فإن نسبة أحد المثلين إليه كنسبته إلى المثل الثاني، فإذا خصص أحد المثلين دون الثاني علم أن الإيجاب الذاتي باطل، فإن منعوا تلك الوجوه فلا شك أن الجايز بنفسه يتساوى طرفاه ونسبة من حيث هو ذات إلى أحد طرفيه كنسبته إلى الطرف الثاني، فإذا تخصص الذات بالوجود دون العدم فلا بد من مخصص وراء كونه ذاتا وبطل الإيجاب بالذات. والثالث : أن الموجب بالذات ما لم يناسب الموجب بوجه من وجوه المناسبة لم يحصل الموجب، وذلك أنا إذا تصورنا ذاتين أو أمرين معلومين لا اتصال لأحدهما بالآخر ولا مناسبة بينهما ولا تعلق ولا إشعار، بل اختص كل واحد منهما بحقيقته
The first of them: that existence insofar as it is existence is a matter common to the necessary and the contingent, and it is in both one meaning that does not differ. So if He were to bring into existence insofar as He is existence or insofar as He is essence, then no one of the existents would be more deserving of being brought into existence than another. Hence it is established that He brings into existence because He is existence qualified by a quality or an essence qualified by a quality. The second matter indicates that: that the aspects of contingency have entered upon actions, and those aspects are similar. The necessitator (al-mūjib) does not specify one similar for another similar, for the relation of one similar to it is like its relation to the second similar. So if it specifies one similar rather than the other, it is known that intrinsic necessitation (al-ījāb al-dhātī) is false. And if they deny those aspects, then without doubt the contingent in itself has its two sides equal, and the relation of itself as essence to one of its two sides is like its relation to the second side. So if the essence becomes specified by existence rather than non-existence, then there must be a specifier beyond its being an essence, and intrinsic necessitation is false. The third: that the necessitator by essence, as long as it does not have a suitability (munāsaba) with the necessitated in some aspect of suitability, the necessitated does not occur. That because when we conceive of two essences or two known matters with no connection between them, no suitability, no relation, and no indication, but each one is specified by its own reality...
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of 'ījāb dhātī' (intrinsic necessitation) — the idea that the First Cause necessarily produces the world by its essence alone, without will. The author gives three arguments. First argument: Existence is a common term; if the Cause acted by virtue of existence or essence alone, then all existents would be equally produced, which is not the case. Therefore, the Cause must act by virtue of a specific attribute or will. Second argument: The aspects of contingency (such as temporal priority, spatial location, etc.) are similar; if the Cause necessitated by essence, it would produce all equally, but it specifies some effects, so it must have will. If one denies these aspects, the contingent itself has equal sides; specification requires a specifier beyond essence. Third argument: For a cause to produce an effect, there must be a suitability (munāsaba) between them; if two entities are completely unrelated, one cannot produce the other. This refutes the idea that the world necessarily emanates from God without any relation or will. The argument is cut off but the point is clear.

Key Terms
وجود
ذات
موجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if existence is common, then God must create everything equally.
Correction: The argument actually shows that since not all possibilities are realized, there must be a specifying factor beyond essence.

Checkpoint
What does the first argument conclude about the basis of God's creative act?
A) It is based on existence alone.
B) It is based on essence alone.
C) It is based on existence or essence qualified by a quality.
D) It is based on contingency.

Key Takeaway
The commonality of existence proves that specification requires a qualifying attribute, not mere essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is not a neutral fact—it is a specific choice by the Qualified Essence. Does this realization change how you view your purpose today? What would it mean to live as a 'chosen' being whose very existence demands a response?
?
If God's creation were necessary, you would have no personal accountability. But since it is volitional, you are a creature of will as well. How does knowing you were 'specified' by God's attribute of will affect your attitude toward your own choices? What will you do differently this week?
?
The argument forces you to see that existence alone cannot explain 'why you and not another'. When you look in the mirror, do you see a random possibility or a deliberate act of divine distinction? What thanks does that distinction require from you today?

Second Argument: Similarity of Contingent Aspects
The second argument: the similarity of aspects and the need for a specifier for the contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the similarity of contingent aspects forces the rejection of intrinsic necessitation and requires a specifier beyond essence.

Core Idea
Similar effects require a cause that can distinguish between equals.

The second argument builds on the first by examining the 'aspects of contingency' that characterize possible actions. Sanusi argues that these aspects are similar, and a mere essence cannot prefer one over another. If it does, then intrinsic necessitation is false. He also presents a separate argument: the contingent itself has equal sides, so its specification to existence demands an external specifier.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the second passage carefully. Notice the two parts: first about similar aspects, then about the contingent's equal sides.
The argument uses the concept of 'وجوه الجواز' (aspects of contingency).
2
Understand the logic of similar aspects
If the cause necessitated by essence, it would have to specify one similar aspect over another, which is impossible for a blind necessary cause. But we see such specification, so necessity is false.
The key phrase: 'الموجب لا يخصص مثلا عن مثل'.
3
Understand the alternative reasoning
If someone denies these aspects, then consider the contingent itself: it has two equal sides (existence and non-existence). Its essence alone cannot tip the balance, so a specifier beyond essence is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Third Argument: The Need for Suitability
The third argument: a necessitator by essence requires suitability with its effect; otherwise the effect cannot arise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a cause must have some suitability with its effect, and how this refutes the claim that God necessarily produces the world without any connection.

Core Idea
No suitability, no causation.

The third argument shifts focus to the relationship between cause and effect. Sanusi argues that for a cause to produce an effect, there must be a 'suitability' (munāsaba) between them. If two entities are completely unrelated, one cannot necessitate the other. This undermines the notion of an impersonal necessary emanation from God to the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic passage for the third argument. It is incomplete but the core idea is clear.
The term 'مناسبة' (suitability) is crucial.
2
Understand the concept of suitability
A cause must have some connection or likeness to its effect, like fire and heat. Without any relation, causation is impossible.
Sanusi describes two essences with 'لا اتصال ولا مناسبة'.
3
Apply to God and the world
If God were a necessary cause by essence, He and the world would be completely independent, yet He produces it. This requires a relation. The relation is God's will and wisdom, not brute necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Objection: Does Being Caused Entail Temporal Origination?
Objection and initial answer about causation and temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that being caused does not necessarily imply temporal origination, and the beginning of the response from the Ash'ari perspective.

Core Idea
Dependence on a cause does not automatically require a beginning in time.

This lesson presents a key objection raised against the Ash'ari argument that the world's contingency proves it was created in time. The objector grants that the world needs a cause but questions why that cause must bring it about after non-existence. The passage includes the beginning of the answer, which aims to clarify the language of causation and removes false implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the objector initially concedes the world's contingency.
2
Identify the concession and the challenge
The objector says 'أما كون العالم جايز الوجود واحتياجه إلى واجب الوجود فمسلم' — he concedes that the world is possible and needs a cause. Then he asks: why does being existent through another necessitate temporal origination?
The core challenge: eternal dependence is possible.
3
Examine the argument about non-existence as a condition
The objector examines the agreed case of creation from non-existence and questions whether prior non-existence is a condition for causation. He argues that non-existence has no effect; the thing depends only on the cause as cause.
This leads to the possibility of an eternally caused existence.
4
Read the beginning of the answer
The answer starts by saying we must strip away illusions from language. The phrase 'from non-existence' might wrongly make non-existence seem like a real thing with priority. The answer cuts off; think about what might follow.
The answer is incomplete in this passage.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this objection matters for the proofs of the world's temporal origination. Without a response, the argument from contingency alone would not prove creation in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: أما كون العالم جايز الوجود واحتياجه إلى واجب الوجود فمسلم، لكن لم قلتم: إن كونه موجوداً بغيره يستدعي حدوثه عن عدم، فإن وجود الشيء بالشيء لا ينافي كونه دايم الوجود به، وإنما يتبين هذا بأن نبحث في الموضع المتفق عليه أنه إذا أحدثه عن عدم، هل كان سبق العدم شرطًا في تحقق الإحداث؟ قلنا: لا يجوز أن يكون شرطًا، فإن المحدث ما استند إلى المحدث إلا من حيث فقط والعدم لا تأثير له في صحة الإيجاد، فيجوز أن يكون دايم الوجود وجوده بغيره.

والجواب: قلنا: الواجب أن نزيل عن الكلام ما يوقعه الوهم حتى يتجرد المعقول الصرف عن العقل، فقول القائل: وجد عن عدم، أو بعد العدم، أو سبقه العدم إن كان يعني به أن العدم شيء يتحقق له سبق وتقدم وتأخر، واستمرار
If it is said: As for the world being possible of existence and needing the Necessary of Existence, that is conceded. But why do you say that its being existent through another necessitates its temporal origination from non-existence? For the existence of a thing through another does not preclude it being eternal in existence through it. This can only be clarified by examining the agreed-upon point: if He brings it into existence from non-existence, was the priority of non-existence a condition for the actualization of bringing into existence? We say: it is not permissible to be a condition, for the brought-into-existence is only dependent on the bringer-qua-bringer, and non-existence has no effect on the validity of bringing into existence. So it is possible for it to be eternal in existence, its existence through another.

And the answer: We say: It is necessary to remove from the discourse what illusion casts upon it so that pure rationality is stripped for the intellect. So the one who says: it came to be from non-existence, or after non-existence, or non-existence preceded it—if he means by that that non-existence is something that has priority, anteriority, posteriority, and continuation...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is an objection and the beginning of an answer. The objector agrees that the world is contingent and needs a cause, but questions whether being caused necessarily implies that it came into existence after a state of non-existence (temporal origination, huduth). He argues that something can be eternally dependent on another, like the relationship between the intellect and its object. He challenges that prior non-existence is not a condition for causation; rather, causation is what it is regardless of non-existence. The answer starts by stating that we must remove the illusions of common language. The phrase 'came to be from non-existence' may wrongly imply that non-existence is a real entity with temporal priority. The answer cuts off here, but likely continues to explain that non-existence is nothing, and that being from another does not require it to have been preceded by non-existence; eternal dependence is possible, as the Ash'ari position on the world's temporal origination would need further proof beyond mere dependence.

Key Terms
حدوث
قدم
وجود بالغير
العدم
الإحداث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contingency directly proves creation in time.
Correction: The objector shows that dependence does not necessarily entail temporal origination; eternal dependence is possible. Extra argument is needed to prove that the world began.

Checkpoint
What does the objector concede in this passage?
A) The world is eternal.
B) The world is contingent and needs a cause.
C) The world is uncaused.
D) The world is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the assumption that contingency implies temporal origination, showing that eternal dependence is logically possible.

4 min
God's Transcendence Negates Essential Necessitation
Ash'ari denies essential necessitation based on God's transcendence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari argues that essential necessitation is unintelligible given God's absolute oneness and transcendence.

Core Idea
God's transcendence makes any necessary connection from His essence impossible.

Ash'ari begins by establishing God's unique reality, free from any relations or attachments. From this, he deduces that nothing can be forced to exist from God by His essence. Let's examine this foundational premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the emphasis on God's transcendence.
The passage uses strong language: 'exalted and transcendent'.
2
Identify God's attributes mentioned
List the attributes: unique in reality, necessary existence, no second attribute. How do these support the argument?
3
Reflect on why essential necessitation requires a relation
Ask yourself: if God is completely transcendent, can any relation or connection exist? Why would that make essential necessitation unintelligible?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخاصته لم يقض العقل بصدور أحدهما عن الثاني، وواجب الوجود لذاته ذات قد تعالى وتقدس عن جميع وجوه المناسبات والتعلق والاتصال، بل هو منفرد بحقيقته التي هي له وهي وجوب وجوده ولا صفة له ثانية تزيد على ذاته الواجبة، فلا يلزم أن يوجد عنه شيء بحكم الذات، فإيجاب الذات غير معقول أصلا بعد رفع النسب والعلائق، وعن هذا قلتم : إن الموجب لما كان واحدا استحال أن يصدر عنه شيئان معا، ولما كان عقلاً أي : مجردًا عن المادة أوجب عقلا بالفعل مجردًا عن المادة، ولما اقتضى الإيجاب مناسبة والمناسبة تقتضي المماثلة حتى ينوب أحد المثلين مناب الثاني ؛ ثبت لواجب الوجود مثل ثبوت مناب واجب الوجود في الإيجاب، فعرف أن الإيجاب الذاتي باطل، فتعين الإيجاد الاختياري، وذلك ما أردنا أن نورد.
And its particular ruling: the intellect does not judge that one of them proceeds from the other. And the Necessary Existence in Himself is an essence—exalted and transcendent above all aspects of fitness, attachment, and connection. Rather, He is unique in His reality, which is His and is the necessity of His existence, and He has no second attribute additional to His necessary essence. So it is not necessary that anything exist from Him by virtue of essence. Therefore, essential necessitation is completely unintelligible after removing relations and connections. And on this basis you said: Since the necessitator is one, it is impossible that two things proceed from it simultaneously. And since it is an intellect—i.e., abstracted from matter—it necessitates an intellect in act abstracted from matter. And since necessitation requires fitness, and fitness requires resemblance so that one of two similars can substitute for the other, then it would be established that the Necessary Existence has a like—which would substitute for the Necessary Existence in necessitation. Thus it is known that essential necessitation is false, so voluntary creation is determined. That is what we intended to present.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing that essential necessitation (al-ijab al-dhati) is false, and therefore creation must be voluntary (ikhtiyari). The author begins by stating that the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud) is absolutely one and transcendent, devoid of any relations, attachments, or additional attributes. Consequently, nothing can proceed from Him by necessity of essence, because essential necessitation would imply a relationship or connection, which is incompatible with His transcendence. The author then presents an argument based on the oneness of the ground: if the necessitator is one, only one effect can proceed; yet we observe multiple effects, so the necessitator cannot necessitate by essence. Similarly, if the ground were an intellect separate from matter, it would necessitate only one intellect in act. Furthermore, necessitation requires fitness (munasaba) between cause and effect, and fitness entails resemblance (mumathala), allowing one to substitute for the other. But the Necessary Being has no likeness, so no such resemblance exists. Therefore, essential necessitation is impossible, and we conclude that creation must be voluntary—an act of free choice (ikhtiyar).

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
إيجاب ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Essential necessitation means God's essence forces creation in a way compatible with His transcendence.
Correction: Ash'ari argues that essential necessitation would imply a relation between God and creation, contradicting His absolute transcendence and oneness.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, why is 'essential necessitation' unintelligible?
A) Because God has multiple attributes.
B) Because God is transcendent and free from any relations or connections.
C) Because only one effect can proceed from a single cause.
D) Because necessity requires a like to God.

Key Takeaway
Essential necessitation is false because God is absolutely one and free from any relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or perform good deeds, do you subconsciously expect specific outcomes from God as if He owes you? How does knowing that nothing proceeds from God 'by virtue of essence' challenge that expectation, and what will you do this week to surrender that entitlement?
?
Think of a time you felt God 'should' have responded to you in a certain way because of your sincerity. How does the truth that God is free from all necessity and relation change how you view that experience? Will you let go of that expectation today?
?
If God's actions are not necessitated by any attribute, then your relationship with Him is not based on a formula. Are you comfortable with a God who is utterly free, or do you secretly wish He were more predictable? How will you respond to His absolute freedom in your next decision?

Arguments from Oneness and Resemblance for Voluntary Creation
Ash'ari uses arguments from oneness and resemblance to refute essential necessitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ash'ari's arguments from the oneness of the necessitator and the requirement of resemblance, leading to the conclusion of voluntary creation.

Core Idea
Multiple effects from a single necessary cause prove that creation is an act of free will.

Having established that essential necessitation is unintelligible in itself, Ash'ari now refutes it with two specific arguments: the oneness of the cause would produce only one effect, and necessitation requires resemblance which is impossible for God. These reinforce the conclusion that creation is voluntary.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the two distinct arguments Ash'ari presents.
The arguments are introduced with 'وعن هذا قلتم' and 'ولما اقتضى'.
2
Understand the argument from oneness
Ash'ari argues that if the necessitator is one, only one effect can proceed. Since we observe many effects, the cause cannot necessitate by essence.
This argument relies on the principle of 'one cause, one effect'.
3
Understand the argument from resemblance
Necessitation requires a fitness (munasaba) between cause and effect, which entails resemblance. If God necessitated, then something would resemble Him, which is impossible.
Resemblance would allow substitution, implying a like to God.
4
Conclude voluntary creation
Since both arguments show essential necessitation is false, the only remaining possibility is that creation is voluntary (ikhtiyari).
This is the final conclusion of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objection and Answer: Does Dependence Imply Temporal Origination?
Ash'ari answers the objection that dependence does not require temporal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari answers the objection that the world's dependence on God does not require temporal origination, and why essential priority is sufficient.

Core Idea
Dependence on God means essential priority, not temporal priority.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a key objection: if the world depends on God, why must it have been created from non-existence? Could it not be eternal while depending on Him? The answer reveals a deep insight about the role of imagination in conceiving time and establishes that the world's origination is about essential priority, not a temporal gap.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the challenge: the objector concedes the world's contingency but questions why dependence implies a beginning from non-existence. Note the objector's suggestion that existence by another could be eternal.
The objector is not denying dependence, only that it necessitates a temporal start.
2
Examine the answer's critique of imagination
The author argues that the notion of non-existence having temporal priority is an illusion of the imagination (wahm). Just as we imagine a void beyond the world, we imagine a time before the world, but this is not real.
The author uses analogies of space and time to show the illusion.
3
Grasp the key move: essential priority
Once the illusion is removed, the author explains that 'origination from non-existence' means that the world has a beginning in the sense that its existence depends on a cause that is prior to it in essence and existence, not in time.
This priority is called 'bil-dhat wa al-wujud' (by essence and existence).
4
Apply the principle to the world
The world is possible in itself, so its existence requires a cause. That cause (God) is prior in essence. This is the meaning of origination: the world was not, then was, without implying a temporal interval.
The author concludes that the world is temporally originated only in the sense of essential priority, not time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : أما كون العالم جايز الوجود واحتياجه إلى واجب الوجود فمسلم، لكن لم قلتم : إن كونه موجوداً بغيره يستدعي حدوثه عن عدم، فإن وجود الشيء بالشيء لا ينافي كونه دايم الوجود به، وإنما يتبين هذا بأن نبحث في الموضع المتفق أنه إذا أحدثه عن عدم، هل كان سبق العدم شرطًا في تحقق الإحداث؟ قلنا : لا يجوز أن يكون شرطًا، فإن المحدث ما استند إلى المحدث إلا من حيث فقط والعدم لا تأثير له في صحة الإيجاد، فيجوز أن يكون دايم الوجود وجوده بغيره. والجواب : قلنا : الواجب أن نزيل عن الكلام ما يوقعه الوهم حتى يتجرد المعقول الصرف عن العقل، فقول القائل : وجد عن عدم، أو بعد العدم، أو سبقه العدم إن كان يعني به أن العدم شيء يتحقق له سبق وتقدم وتأخر، واستمرار وانقطاع أو شيء يوجد عنه شيء ، فذلك كله من إيهام الخيال حيث لم يمكنه شيء، تصور الأولية في الشيء الحادث إلا مستندا إلى شيء موهوم كالزمان والمدة، كما لم يمكنه تصور النهاية في العالم إلا مستندا إلى شيء موهوم كالخلا والفضا، وكما لم يمكن فرض خلاء بين وجود الباري تعالى وبين العالم، لا يمكن أيضا فرض زمان وتقدير زمان بين وجود الباري تعالى وبين العالم، فإن ذلك من عمل الوهم فقط، ولا يلزم منه المعية بالزمان، كما لا يلزم المعية من المكان. فافهم ذلك فيجب أن تزيل هذا الإيهام عن فكرك، فيتصور أن وجود شيء لا من شيء هو المعني بحدوث الشيء عن العدم، فإن قولنا : له أول المعنى بحدوثه، وإن قولنا : لم يكن فكان هو المعنى بسبق العدم ؛ إذ لا بد من عبارة وتوسع في الكلام ليطلق لفظ السبق والتأخر والاستمرار والانقطاع ، وإذا ثبتت هذه القاعدة، فتقول : إذا كان العالم ممكن الوجود باعتبار ذاته، والممكن معناه أنه جايز الوجود وجايز العدم، فيستوي طرفاه أعني : الوجود والعدم باعتبار ذاته ، فإذا وجد فإنما يوجد باعتبار موجده ، ولولا موجده لما استحق إلا العدم، فهو إذا مستحق الوجود والعدم بالاعتبارين المذكورين، فكان واجب الوجود سابقا عليه بالذات والوجود ؛ إذ لولاه لما وجد. ولا يجوز أن يكون وجوده مع واجب الوجود بالذات والوجود جميعا ؛ لأن قبل ومع بالذات والوجود لا يجتمعان في شيء واحد، فهو إذا متأخر الوجود، ولا يجوز أن يكون مع واجب الوجود بالزمان؛ لأنه يوجب أن يكون واجب الوجود زمانيا ؛ لأن قولنا : مع من جملة المتضايفات كالأخوة والأبوة، فأحد الشيئين إذا
For it might be said: As for the world being possible of existence and its need for the Necessary Existence, that is conceded. But why do you say that its being existent by another necessitates its origination from non-existence? For the existence of a thing by another does not preclude its being eternal in existence by it. This can only be clarified by investigating the agreed-upon case: when He originated it from non-existence, was the precedence of non-existence a condition for the actualization of origination? We say: It is not allowed that it be a condition. For the originated thing is related to the originator only from the aspect of its existence, and non-existence has no effect on the validity of bringing into existence. So it is possible that it be eternal in existence, its existence being by another. The answer: We say: It is necessary to remove from the discourse what the imagination (wahm) casts into it, so that pure reason becomes abstracted from the intellect. So one who says: 'It came from non-existence, or after non-existence, or non-existence preceded it'—if he means by that that non-existence is a thing that has priority, posteriority, continuation, and discontinuation, or a thing from which something comes, then all that is from the illusion of the imagination, since it cannot conceive of priority in an originated thing except by leaning on an imaginary thing like time and duration, just as it cannot conceive of an end in the world except by leaning on an imaginary thing like void and space. And just as it is impossible to suppose a void between the existence of the Exalted Creator and the world, it is also impossible to suppose time or temporal measure between the existence of the Exalted Creator and the world. For that is only the work of the imagination, and it does not entail simultaneity in time, just as it does not entail simultaneity in place. So understand that. Therefore, you must remove this illusion from your thought, so that it is conceived that the existence of a thing from no thing is what is meant by the origination of a thing from non-existence. For our saying 'it has a beginning' is the meaning of its origination, and our saying 'it was not, then it was' is the meaning of the precedence of non-existence; for there must be verbal expression and extension in speech to allow the use of the terms priority, posteriority, continuation, and discontinuation. And when this principle is established, we say: Since the world is possible of existence with respect to its essence, and the possible means that it is possible of existence and possible of non-existence, so its two ends—namely existence and non-existence—are equal with respect to its essence. So if it exists, it exists only by virtue of its originator, and were it not for its originator, it would deserve only non-existence. So it deserves existence and non-existence by the two mentioned aspects. Thus the Necessary Existence was prior to it in essence and existence; for without Him it would not exist. And it is not possible that its existence be together with the Necessary Existence in both essence and existence, because priority and togetherness in essence and existence cannot coexist in a single thing. So it is posterior in existence. And it is not possible that it be together with the Necessary Existence in time, because that would require that the Necessary Existence be temporal. For our saying 'together' is one of the correlatives (mutadāyifāt), like brotherhood and fatherhood; so one of the two things, if...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector grants that the world is contingent but questions why dependence on another necessitates origination from non-existence; could it not be eternal while depending on God? The objector suggests that the very notion of 'origination from non-existence' might imply that non-existence is a precondition, which the objector argues is not necessary. The answer begins by clarifying a fundamental illusion: the imagination (wahm) projects a temporal interval between God and the world, as if non-existence were a real entity with priority. The author explains that this is an imaginative construct, similar to how we imagine a void beyond the world or a time before the world. In reality, there is no time or void between the Creator and creation. When we speak of 'from non-existence' or 'before', we are using language metaphorically to indicate that the thing has a beginning and was not then was. Once this illusion is removed, the author establishes the real argument: the world is possible in itself, so it is inherently equal in relation to existence and non-existence. Its existence must come from its Creator. Therefore, the Creator is prior to it in essence and existence (bil-dhat wa al-wujud)—that is, without the Creator, the world would not exist. This priority is not temporal, because temporal priority would make the Creator subject to time, which is impossible. Thus, the world is temporally originated in the sense of being preceded by non-existence in the order of essence, not in time.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'origination from non-existence' implies a temporal gap between God and the world.
Correction: The author shows that origination means essential priority: God is prior in essence and existence, not in time. The language of 'before' and 'after' is metaphorical.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does it mean for the world to be originated from non-existence?
A) It came after a time when it did not exist.
B) It is preceded by God in essence and existence, not in time.
C) There was a void of time before it.
D) It depends on God but could be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The world's origination from non-existence means that God is prior to it in essence and existence, not that there was a time before the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been taught that God created the world 'in time.' But the Ash'ari argues that God's priority is essential, not temporal. If you removed the illusion of time from your conception of creation, how would your daily awareness of God's presence change? Do you still imagine a distant God who acted long ago, or can you see that your very existence depends on Him at every moment? Commit to recognizing today that you exist only by God's essential priority.
?
The author says the imagination cannot conceive of priority without leaning on an imaginary time. In your own life, when you think about your relationship with God, do you often imagine a gap—a 'time' when you were far from Him? How might this illusion of a temporal gap be preventing you from feeling His immediate, constant dependence? What would it mean for you today to act as if God's priority over you is essential, not just historical?
?
You might think that your sins are in the past, separated from God by time. But the Ash'ari teaches that non-existence is not a thing. Does that mean your past sins are not 'there' as a barrier between you and God? If God's priority is essential, and He is always present, then your repentance is not reaching across time but rather turning your essence toward Him. Ask yourself: Are you using the illusion of time as an excuse to delay repentance?

6 min
The World's Beginning from Its Possibility
Proof that the world has a beginning through possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's dependence on the Creator is through possibility, not existence, and how this proves the world has a beginning.

Core Idea
Possibility, not existence, is what ties the world to its Creator.

In this passage, the author refutes a common objection: that the world depends on God only by virtue of its existence. He argues that this would lead to infinite regress. Instead, he establishes that the world depends on God from the aspect of its possibility, which is a distinct quality that precedes existence. This leads to the conclusion that the world has a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the objection
Begin by reading the passage carefully. Note the initial claim attributed to the opponent: that the world depends on the Creator only by virtue of its existence. Ponder why someone might think that.
The opponent's view seems logical at first glance.
2
Follow the refutation
The author responds with a conditional argument: if dependence were only through existence, then every existent would depend on God, leading to an infinite regress without reaching the Necessary Being. See how this is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Identify the alternative and key concepts
The author proposes that dependence is through possibility (jawāz). Understand the three terms: possibility, existence, non-existence. Possibility is a distinct 'matter' beyond existence and non-existence. Grasp the precedence claim: possibility precedes existence essentially.
Key terms: جواز (possibility), ذاتي (intrinsic), عرضي (accidental).
4
Study the argument for the world's beginning
The author then shows that the contingent, in itself, deserves non-existence without its Creator. Therefore, it is preceded by the existence of the Necessary and by its own non-existence. This establishes a beginning (awwal) for the world.
The conclusion is that it is impossible for what has a beginning to be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم: إن العالم في المحل المتفق عليه إنما يستند إلى الموجد لمن حيث وجوده فقط، والعدم لا تأثير له في صحة الإيجاد، قلنا: ولو استند إلى الموجد من حيث وجوده فقط لاستند كل موجود وتسلسل القول إلى ما لا يتناهى، ولم يستند إلى واجب وجوده، بل إنما استند إليه من حيث جوازه فقط، والجواز قضية أخرى وراء الوجود والعدم، ثم جواز الوجود سابق على الوجود بالذات. فنقول: إنما وُجد به؛ لأنه كان جايز الوجود، ولا نقول: إنما كان جايز الوجود؛ لأنه وجد، فجواز وجوده ذاتي له، والوجود عرضي، والذاتي سابق على العرضي سبقاً ذاتيا، ثم بينا أن الجايز مستحق العدم باعتبار ذاته لولا موجده فكان مسبوقاً بوجوده، ومسبوقاً بعدم ذاته لولا موجده؛ لأن استحقاق وجوده مأخوذ من الغير، واستحقاق عدمه ذاتي مأخوذ من ذاته، فهو إذا مسبوق بوجود واجب، ومسبوق بعدم جايز، فتحقق له أول، والجمع بين ما له أول وبين ما لا أول له محال.
And as for your saying: 'The world, according to the agreed-upon meaning, only depends on the Creator (al-mūjid) by virtue of its existence alone, and non-existence has no effect on the validity of bringing into existence,' we say: If it depended on the Creator by virtue of its existence alone, then every existent would depend on Him and the argument would regress infinitely, and it would not depend on the Necessary of existence. Rather, it depends on Him only by virtue of its possibility (jawāz). And possibility is another matter beyond existence and non-existence. Then the possibility of existence precedes existence essentially. So we say: It came into existence by Him only because it was possible of existence, and we do not say: It was possible of existence only because it existed. For the possibility of its existence is intrinsic (dhātī) to it, while existence is accidental (ʿaraḍī). And the intrinsic precedes the accidental by an essential precedence. Then we showed that the contingent is deserving of non-existence by virtue of itself, were it not for its Creator, so it is preceded by its existence (i.e., the Creator's existence) and preceded by the non-existence of itself, were it not for its Creator, because the entitlement to its existence is taken from another, while the entitlement to its non-existence is intrinsic, taken from itself. Thus it is preceded by the existence of the Necessary and preceded by the non-existence of the contingent. Hence a beginning (awwal) is established for it, and to combine what has a beginning with what has no beginning is impossible.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the world has a beginning (awwal) by analyzing the nature of its dependence on the Creator. It refutes the claim that the world depends on God only by virtue of its existence. The author argues that if that were the case, then every existent would depend on God in the same way, leading to an infinite regress without reaching the Necessary Being. Instead, the world depends on God from the aspect of its possibility (jawāz), which is a distinct quality separate from existence and non-existence. Possibility of existence precedes existence essentially. The author clarifies: the world exists because it was possible, not that it became possible because it exists. Possibility is intrinsic (dhātī) to the contingent, while existence is accidental (ʿaraḍī). The intrinsic necessarily precedes the accidental. Then the author establishes that the contingent, in itself, deserves non-existence without its Creator. Therefore, the contingent is preceded both by the existence of the Necessary (its cause) and by its own non-existence. This proves the contingent has a beginning, and it is impossible for something with a beginning to be eternal.

Refuting Dependence from Existence Alone
Refutation of objection: world depends on Creator from possibility, not existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's dependence on the Creator must be grounded in possibility rather than existence alone, thereby avoiding infinite regress.

Core Idea
Dependence from existence alone leads to infinite regress; possibility is the true ground.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection: If the world depends on the Creator only because of its existence, then every existing thing would require the same, leading to an infinite chain. The author shows that the world depends on the Creator because of its possibility, a property distinct from existence. This argument establishes the need for a Creator while preserving the uniqueness of the Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's claim and the author's immediate rebuttal about infinite regress.
The author uses a conditional: 'if only from existence, then...'
2
Understand the alternative: possibility
Identify the shift from existence to possibility. Note that possibility (جواز) is a separate property that grounds the dependence.
Possibility is defined as 'another matter beyond existence and non-existence'.
3
Grasp the essential precedence
Understand the argument that possibility is essential (ذاتي) and existence is accidental (عرضي), so possibility precedes existence essentially.
This establishes that the possible being has a beginning (first).
4
Connect to the Necessary Existent
See how the possible being is preceded by the Creator's existence and its own non-existence, making it contingent and thus requiring a Necessary Being.
The conclusion: combining something with a first and something without a first is impossible.

Rather, it depends on Him only from the aspect of its possibility, and possibility is another matter beyond existence and non-existence. Then we showed that the possible deserves non-existence by virtue of its essence were it not for its creator, so it is preceded by His existence, and preceded by the non-existence of its essence were it not for its creator, because the deserving of its existence is taken from another, and the deserving of its non-existence is essential taken from its essence. Thus it is preceded by the existence of a Necessary Being, and preceded by the non-existence of a possible, so it has a first established, and combining what has a first with what has no first is impossible.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific objection. The objector claims that the world depends on the Creator only from the aspect of its existence, not from non-existence. The author replies that if it depended only from existence, then every existent would depend on the Creator in the same way, leading to an infinite regress of causes, and no existent would trace back to a Necessary Existent. Instead, the dependence is from the aspect of possibility, which is a quality distinct from existence and non-existence. He further argues that the possible being deserves non-existence by its own essence, so it is preceded by the Creator's existence and by its own non-existence—thus it has a beginning (first), and combining that with something that has no beginning (the Necessary Being) is impossible. The argument guards against confusing accidental existence with essential possibility.

Existence vs. Necessity: What the Possible Being Receives from God
Clarification: effect receives existence, not necessity; necessity is concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle distinction between a possible thing's existence coming from God and its necessity being a concomitant attribute, not directly imparted.

Core Idea
The possible being receives existence, not necessity; necessity arises only when the cause is considered.

The objection challenges whether saying 'the world is necessary by God's necessitation' differs from saying 'it exists by His bringing into existence.' The answer reveals a crucial nuance: the possible is defined solely by existence and non-existence, not necessity. This distinction safeguards God's agency from being reduced to a mechanical necessitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection in Arabic and translation. Notice how the objector conflates 'existence by another' with 'necessity by another' and gives the hand-key example.
The objector assumes that if something exists by another, it becomes necessary by that other.
2
Identify the key distinction in the answer
The answer starts by defining the possible: it is possible of existence and non-existence, not possible of necessity and impossibility. This is the foundation.
Possible is defined by existence/non-existence, not by necessity.
3
See what the possible acquires from the preponderator
The author states clearly: 'It only acquires from the preponderator its existence, not its necessity.' This is the central claim.
4
Understand necessity as a concomitant ('arad)
Necessity occurs to the possible when the cause is considered, but it is not directly given by the cause. The cause imparts existence, and necessity is a label that follows.
Necessity is an attribute that arises after existence, not a direct gift.
5
Reflect on the theological import
This distinction preserves that God gives existence without making His action necessary. The effect depends on God for its existence, not for necessity.
Avoids theologically problematic necessitarianism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فما الفرق بين وجوب العالم بإيجاب الباري تعالى وبين وجوده بإيجاد الباري تعالى، فإن العالم إذا كان ممكنا في ذاته ووجد بغيره فقد وجب به، وهذا حكم كل علة ومعلول، وسبب ومسبب، فإن المسبب أبدا يجب بالسبب فيكون جايزا باعتبار ذاته، واجباً باعتبار سببه، ثم السبب يتقدم المسبب بالذات وإن كانا معا في الوجود كما تقول: تحركت يدي فتحرك المفتاح في كمي، ولا يمكنك أن تقول: تحرك المفتاح في كمي فتحركت يدي، وإن كانت الحركتان معا في الوجود؟ والجواب: قلنا: وجود الشيء بإيجاد موجده صواب من حيث اللفظ والمعنى، بخلاف وجوب الشيء بإيجاب الموجب وذلك أن الممكن معناه أنه جايز وجوده وجايز عدمه، لا جايز وجوبه وجايز امتناعه، وإنما استفاد من المرجح وجوده لا وجوبه، نعم لما وجد عرض له الوجوب عند ملاحظة السبب؛ لأن السبب إفادة الوجوب حتى يقال: وجب بإيجابه ثم عرض له الوجوب بل أفاده الوجود، فصح أن يقال: وجد بإيجاده وعرض له الوجود فانتسب إليه وجوده؛ إذ كان ممكن الوجود لا ممكن الوجوب، وهذه دقيقة لطيفة لا بد من مراعاتها.
For it is said: What is the difference between the necessity of the world by the necessitation of the Most High and its existence by the bringing into existence of the Most High? For if the world is possible in itself and exists by another, then it becomes necessary by it, and this is the rule of every cause and effect, and every ground and consequent. For the consequent always becomes necessary by the ground, so it is possible by virtue of its essence and necessary by virtue of its cause, and then the ground precedes the consequent in essence even if they are simultaneous in existence, as you say: 'My hand moved, so the key moved in my sleeve,' and you cannot say: 'The key moved in my sleeve, so my hand moved,' even if the two movements are simultaneous in existence. The answer: We say: The existence of a thing by its creator's bringing into existence is correct in word and meaning, unlike the necessity of a thing by the necessitation of the necessitator. That is because the possible means that it is possible of existence and possible of non-existence, not possible of necessity and possible of impossibility. It only acquires from the preponderator its existence, not its necessity. Indeed, when it exists, necessity occurs to it when the cause is considered, because the cause imparts necessity, so it is said: 'It became necessary by His necessitation,' then necessity occurred to it, but rather He imparted existence. So it is correct to say: 'It existed by His bringing into existence' and existence occurred to it, so its existence is attributed to Him, since it is possible of existence, not possible of necessity. This is a subtle point that must be observed.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector asks: What distinguishes saying 'the world is necessary by God's necessitation' from saying 'it exists by God's bringing into existence'? The objector argues that if something is possible in itself and exists by another, it becomes necessary by that other, just as any effect becomes necessary by its cause. He gives the example of hand and key movements. The answer clarifies that the possible is defined as possible of existence and possible of non-existence, not possible of necessity and possible of impossibility. The preponderator (God) imparts existence, not necessity. Necessity is a concomitant attribute that arises when the cause is considered, but it is not directly given. Therefore, it is correct to say 'it exists by His bringing into existence' and then existence occurs to it, making its existence attributed to Him. This is a subtle distinction that must be upheld to avoid attributing necessity directly to the agent.

Correction: The possible being receives only existence from the preponderator. Necessity is a concomitant attribute that arises when the cause is considered, not directly imparted.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A possible being does not receive necessity from its cause; it receives existence, and necessity is a concomitant label applied only when the cause is considered.

?
If necessity is only a label that appears when we consider the cause, then your very existence is a constant gift, not a fixed fact. Are you grateful for this ongoing gift, or have you become numb to the miracle that you exist at all? What one small practice could remind you hourly that you are 'possible of existence'?

Existence vs. Necessity in Causality
The author answers an objection about simultaneous cause and effect by distinguishing existence from necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical distinction between the existence of a thing and its necessity, and how this resolves the objection about simultaneous causality.

Core Idea
Existence is given by the Creator; necessity is merely accidental.

This passage tackles a subtle but crucial point in understanding causality: if cause and effect are simultaneous, how can one be caused by the other? The author answers by distinguishing between the existence (wujud) of an effect and its necessity (wujub). This distinction preserves the causal relationship without requiring temporal priority.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection about two simultaneous movements (hand and key) where the causal direction is fixed. Ask yourself: how can we still say one causes the other if they exist together?
The objector is using a common example to challenge causal priority.
2
Grasp the distinction
The author distinguishes existence (wujud) from necessity (wujub). The possible (mumkin) only receives existence from the preponderator, not necessity. Necessity arises only accidentally after existence.
This is the key conceptual move.
3
Apply the distinction
Notice the correct phrasing: 'existed by His creation' not 'necessarily existed by His necessitation.' The subtle point is that while cause and effect are simultaneous in existence, the causal dependence is on existence alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معا في الوجود كما تقول : تحركت يدي فتحرك المفتاح في كمي، ولا يمكنك أن تقول : تحرك المفتاح في كمي فتحركت يدي، وإن كانت الحركتان معا في الوجود؟ والجواب : قلنا : وجود الشيء بإيجاد موجده صواب من حيث اللفظ والمعنى، بخلاف وجوب الشيء بإيجاب الموجب وذلك أن الممكن معناه أنه جايز وجوده لا جايز وجوبه، وجايز عدمه لا جايز امتناعه، وإنما استفاد من المرجح وجوده لا وجوبه، نعم لما وُجد عرض له الوجوب عند ملاحظة السبب؛ لأن السبب إفادة الوجوب حتى يقال : وجب بإيجابه ثم عرض له الوجوب، بل أفاده الوجود، فصح أن يُقال : وجد بإيجاده وعرض له الوجود فانتسب إليه وجوده؛ إذ كان ممكن الوجود لا ممكن الوجوب، وهذه دقيقة لطيفة لا بد من مراعاتها.
Together in existence, as you say: 'My hand moved, so the key moved in my sleeve,' and you cannot say: 'The key moved in my sleeve, so my hand moved,' even though both movements are together in existence? The answer: We say: The existence of a thing by the creative act of its Creator is correct in wording and meaning, unlike the necessity of a thing by the necessitation of the necessitator. That is because the possible means that its existence is possible, not that its necessity is possible, and its non-existence is possible, not that its impossibility is possible. It only obtains from the preponderator its existence, not its necessity. Yes, when it exists, necessity occurs to it when the cause is considered, because the cause gives necessity such that it is said: 'It became necessary by His necessitation' then necessity occurs to it. Rather, it gives it existence, so it is correct to say: 'It existed by His creation' and existence occurs to it, so its existence is attributed to Him, since it is possible in existence, not possible in necessity. This is a subtle point that must be observed.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding simultaneous cause and effect. The objector claims that two movements can coexist simultaneously, as when the hand moves and the key moves together, yet one cannot reverse the causal order. The author answers by distinguishing between existence (wujud) and necessity (wujub). The possible (mumkin) is defined as that which can exist or not exist, not that it can be necessary or impossible. The preponderator (murajjih) gives existence, not necessity. When the possible exists, it acquires necessity only accidentally ('aradi) in relation to the cause. The author emphasizes that the correct statement is 'found (wujida) by His creation' not 'necessarily existed (wajaba) by His necessitation.' This subtle distinction (daqiqa latifa) must be observed.

Misconception: Assuming that if two things are simultaneous, they are equally caused or uncaused, ignoring the distinction between existence and necessity.
Correction: Simultaneity does not negate causal dependence. The effect receives its existence from the cause, even though they exist together. Necessity is not what is given directly; only existence is.

The Impossibility of Simultaneity Between Eternal and Temporal
The author addresses an objection that a cause can precede in essence but be simultaneous in time with its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal Creator cannot be simultaneous with the temporal world in any sense, and how the author refutes an objection based on cause-effect simultaneity.

Core Idea
The eternal and temporal cannot coexist, even if a cause can be simultaneous with its effect.

A common objection to the Creator's priority is that a cause can precede in essence yet be simultaneous in time with its effect. The author concedes this principle but shows it only applies when both are temporal. He then systematically demonstrates that no form of simultaneity—essence, existence, time, rank, or virtue—can exist between the eternal and the temporal. This passage sharpens our understanding of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage and identify the initial objection: a cause can precede in essence but be simultaneous in time with its effect. Understand this as a potential challenge to the Creator's priority.
The objection uses the example of capacity (istitā‘a) and the act.
2
Follow the author's concession and distinction
Notice that the author grants the logical possibility of such simultaneity, but immediately restricts it to things that are both temporal. This distinction is crucial.
The author says 'if that is conceded' – he is not accepting the objection outright.
3
Identify the five types of simultaneity
The author lists five ways two things can be simultaneous: in essence, existence, time, rank, and virtue. Find these in the Arabic and understand each briefly.
These are the only possible modes of simultaneity; if none apply, then no simultaneity exists.
4
Understand the fundamental reason
Ask yourself: why can't the Creator and the world share any of these? The answer is that there is no proportion (munāsaba) between them. The only relation is that of agent to act, and the agent is always prior.
This reinforces the absolute transcendence of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن سلم ذلك فيجوز أن يسبق السبب المسبب ذاتا ويقارنه زمانًا، فيكون زمان وجودهما واحدًا، وأحدهما سبب والثاني مسبب، كما يقال في الاستطاعة مع الفعل، لكن الممتنع وجود ما له أول مع وجود ما لا أول له، وقد بينا أن هذه المعية ممتنعة على الوجود كلها؛ أعني بالذات والوجود والزمان والرتبة والفضيلة، فإن التقدم والتأخر والمعية يطلق على الشيئين إذا كانا متناسبين نوعا من المناسبة، ولا نسبة بين الباري تعالى وبين العالم إلا بوجه الفعل والفاعلية، والفاعل على كل حال متقدم، والمفعول متأخر.
Then, if that is conceded, it is permissible that the cause precedes the effect in essence and is simultaneous with it in time, so that the time of their existence is one, and one is cause and the other effect, as is said about capacity with the act. But the existence of that which has a beginning with that which has no beginning is impossible. And we have shown that this simultaneity is impossible in all aspects of existence; I mean in essence, existence, time, rank, and virtue. For precedence, posteriority, and simultaneity are attributed to two things when they have a proportion of some kind. And there is no proportion between the Exalted Creator and the world except in terms of act and agency. And the agent is in any case prior, and the effect is posterior.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents another objection: a cause can be prior in essence but simultaneous in time with its effect (like capacity and act). The author concedes this but distinguishes that this only applies when both are temporal. It is impossible for a temporal thing to coexist with an eternal thing. The author lists five types of simultaneity (essence, existence, time, rank, virtue) and states that none applies between Creator and creation because there is no proportion. The only relation is that of agent to act, and the agent is always prior.

Misconception: Thinking that God can coexist temporally with the world because cause and effect can be simultaneous in time, ignoring the difference between temporal and eternal.
Correction: The author shows that cause-effect simultaneity only applies when both are temporal. The eternal Creator cannot be simultaneous with the temporal world in any of the five possible aspects.

The Creator, being eternal, cannot be simultaneous with the temporal world in any respect; the only relationship is that of agent to act, with the agent always prior.

?

?
The author says the agent is always prior and the effect always posterior. In what areas of my life am I trying to reverse this order—demanding that God respond to my agenda or be present in my timeframe? What would it look like to truly submit to His priority?

Refuting Necessary Emanation: The Illusion of Causal Necessity
Refutes necessitarian argument through critique of causal necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessitarian argument for the world's necessary emanation from God fails, particularly through the critique of causal necessity.

Core Idea
Causal sequences do not prove necessary emanation; they are merely temporal succession.

This passage tackles one of the most critical philosophical arguments against the Islamic doctrine of creation: the claim that the world emanates from God by necessity. The author, al-Shahrastani, deconstructs the necessitarian analogy between human causal actions and divine creation. By examining the nature of temporal events and causation, he reveals that the supposed 'necessary' connection is nothing more than habit or sequence. Let's read the passage and see how he dismantles this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms like 'إيجاب ذاتي' (necessary emanation) and 'حوادث' (temporal events).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim being refuted
The author is refuting the claim that the world's existence is necessary (wujūb) from God. He argues that existence in temporal events is acquired (mustafād) and not necessary.
3
Understand the analogy critique
The author points out that the only remaining support for the necessitarians is by analogy with natural causation, e.g., hand moving key. He argues that this analogy fails because the opponent may not accept that the hand movement is a cause; it's just sequence.
Notice the key term 'فاء التعقيب' (fa of consequence) indicating sequence, not causation.
4
Examine the matter-form example
The author uses the matter-form analogy: matter is a condition, not a cause; form is given by the Bestower of forms. This further shows that causal necessity is not intrinsic.
This reinforces the point that emanation is not necessary.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Summarize why the necessitarian argument fails: it relies on a false analogy of necessary causation. The author presents an alternative: existence is acquired through divine will and power, not necessity.
You should now be able to articulate the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بإيجاب العلم والإرادة، فإذا تحقق أن الوجود هو المستفاد في الحوادث لا الوجوب بطل الإيجاب الذاتي الذي يحتجوا به حين بنوا عليه مقارنة وجود العالم بوجود الباري تعالى ولم يبق لهم إلا التمثيل بمثال، وذلك من سخف المقال؛ لأن الخصم ربما لا يسلم أن حركة اليد سبب حركة [الكم؟] والمفتاح، ولا يقول بالتولد يبقى مجرد فاء التعقيب والتسبيب لفظا في قوله: تحركت يدي فتحرك المفتاح، وهذا كما أن المادة عند الخصم سبب ما لوجود الصورة حتى يصح أن يقال: لولا المادة لما وجدت الصورة، وهما معا في الوجود وليس وجود الصورة بإيجاد المادة، بل بإيجاد واهب الصور.
By affirming knowledge and will: if it is established that existence is what is acquired in temporal events, not necessity, then the necessary emanation that they argue for is invalidated, which they built upon the comparison of the existence of the world to the existence of God Most High. And they have nothing left but illustration by example, and that is from the foolishness of speech; because the opponent may not concede that the movement of the hand is a cause of the movement of the [sleeve?] and the key, and does not accept generation (tawallud). Nothing remains but the letter fa of consequence and causation in his saying: 'My hand moved, so the key moved.' And this is like how matter according to the opponent is a cause for the existence of form, so that it is valid to say: 'Were it not for matter, form would not exist,' while both coexist in existence; the existence of form is not through the fashioning of matter, but through the fashioning of the Bestower of forms.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the necessitarian argument (al-ījāb al-dhātī) that claims the world emanates necessarily from God. The author argues that the existence of temporal events (al-ḥawādith) is something acquired (mustafād), not necessary (wujūb). Therefore, the comparison between the world's existence and God's existence fails. The only remaining support for the necessitarians is by analogy with natural causation, but this is weak because the opponent may not accept that a hand movement causes the key to move in the way they suppose. The statement 'My hand moved, so the key moved' only indicates sequence (fāʾ al-taʿqīb), not necessary causation. Similarly, the analogy of matter and form: matter is not the efficient cause of form; rather, both coexist and form is given by the Bestower of forms (Wāhib al-ṣuwar). Thus, the necessitarian analogy is invalid.

Key Terms
إيجاب ذاتي
حوادث
تولد
فاء التعقيب
واهب الصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the author denies any form of causality, but he only denies necessary emanation and the analogy to natural causation.
Correction: The author affirms that temporal events have causes in the sense of conditions, but not in a necessitarian sense. Divine will and power are the true causes.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw the author points out in the necessitarian argument?
A) They rely on a false analogy of natural causation.
B) They deny the existence of temporal events.
C) They claim matter creates form.
D) They misuse the term 'existence'.

Key Takeaway
The necessitarian argument collapses because it falsely equates temporal succession with necessary causation; divine creation is by will, not necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe cause and effect in your daily life (e.g., studying leads to grades, effort leads to results), do you ever assume that God is bound by these 'laws'? In what ways might you be treating these sequences as necessities, forgetting that He creates them each moment by His will? What would change in your reliance on Him if you truly saw every outcome as directly from His hand, not from the cause itself?
?
The opponents argued by analogy from natural causation to divine creation. Are there areas in your life where you have unconsciously measured God's actions by human standards? For example, have you ever thought that your good deeds 'cause' His reward, or that your sins 'force' His punishment, rather than seeing them as acts of His wisdom and will? How can you realign your heart to see His will as the only true agent?
?
The author says the only support left for the necessitarian is illustration by example, which is foolish. But we often slip into thinking we can explain God's ways by worldly analogies. When was the last time you tried to fit God into a human box—expecting Him to act according to your understanding of fairness, timing, or causality? Are you willing to abandon those mental constructs and submit to a God who creates by will and knowledge, not by necessity?

Temporal Precedence and Coexistence
Explains cause-effect relation and impossibility of eternal-temporal coexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal cannot coexist with the temporal in any form, and how cause and effect can share time while the cause is prior in essence.

Core Idea
The eternal and temporal cannot coexist; only cause and effect in time share time.

This passage explores the precise relationship between cause and effect, especially when the cause is eternal and the effect temporal. The author explains that while a cause can precede its effect in essence and yet coexist in time (like capacity and act), it is impossible for something that has a beginning to coexist with something that has no beginning — in any respect. This establishes the absolute priority of the Creator over creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the argument structure: concession, exception, enumeration, and final theological point.
The phrase 'على الوجود كلها' is likely a scribal variant for 'على الوجوه كلها' (in all respects).
2
Identify the main claim about cause and effect
The author says a cause can precede in essence (dhātan) and coexist in time (zamānan) with its effect. The example is capacity (istiṭāʿa) and the act. Understand this as a conditional acceptance of causality.
3
Understand the impossibility
The core teaching: something with a beginning cannot coexist with something without a beginning. This impossibility covers five forms: essence, existence, time, rank, and virtue.
These five forms are exhaustive categories of relation.
4
Grasp the theological consequence
Precedence, posteriority, and coexistence only apply when two things share a proportion (munāsaba). Between God and the world, there is only the relation of agent and act, and the agent is always prior. Thus, the world cannot be eternal in any sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن سلم ذلك فيجوز أن يسبق السبب المسبب ذاتا ويقارنه زمانًا، فيكون زمان وجودهما واحدًا، وأحدهما سبب والثاني مسبب، كما يقال في الاستطاعة مع الفعل، لكن الممتنع وجود ما له أول مع وجود ما لا أول له، وقد بينا أن هذه المعية ممتنعة على الوجود كلها؛ أعني بالذات والوجود والزمان والرتبة والفضيلة، فإن التقدم والتأخر والمع يطلق على الشيئين إذا كانا متناسبين نوعا من المناسبة، ولا نسبة بين الباري تعالى وبين العالم إلا بوجه الفعل والفاعلية، والفاعل على كل حال متقدم، والمفعول متأخر.
Then, if that is conceded, it is permissible for the cause to precede the effect in essence and to coexist with it in time, so that the time of their existence is one, and one is cause and the other is effect, as is said concerning capacity (al-istiṭāʿa) with the act. But it is impossible for that which has a beginning to coexist with that which has no beginning. And we have shown that this coexistence is impossible in all respects: I mean in essence, existence, time, rank, and virtue. For precedence, posteriority, and coexistence are predicated of two things when they are proportioned by some proportion. And there is no proportion between God Most High and the world except by the aspect of act and agency (al-fiʿl wa al-fāʿiliyya), and the agent in any case is prior, and the effect is posterior.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage addresses the relationship between cause and effect, and between the eternal and the temporal. The author concedes that if we accept causality, it is possible for a cause to be prior in essence (dhātan) while coexisting in time (zamānan) with its effect, like the capacity (istiṭāʿa) and the act. However, he emphasizes the impossibility of coexistence between something that has a beginning (mā lahu awwal) and something that has no beginning (mā lā awwal lahu). This impossibility applies to all five forms of coexistence: essence (dhāt), existence (wujūd), time (zamān), rank (rutba), and virtue (faḍīla). Precedence, posteriority, and coexistence only apply to things that share a proportion (munāsaba). Between God and the world, the only relationship is that of agent and act (al-fiʿl wa al-fāʿiliyya), and the agent is always prior, the effect posterior. Therefore, the world cannot be eternal in any sense.

ذاتا
زمانا
المعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that coexisting in time implies equal antiquity or a common proportion.
Correction: The author clarifies that priority in essence suffices to prevent the world from being eternal, and that coexistence in time does not imply a proportion between the eternal and temporal.

Checkpoint

A) Essence
B) Time
C) Place
D) Virtue

Key Takeaway
The eternal and temporal cannot coexist in any form—only cause and effect within the temporal realm can share time while the cause is prior in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If nothing temporal can coexist with the Eternal, then your life, which has a beginning, is utterly separate from God's timelessness. Are you living as if your worldly achievements and possessions have any permanence before Him, or do you see them as passing effects of His agency?
?
The passage says there is no proportion between God and the world except act and agency. In your daily actions, do you truly acknowledge God as the sole Agent, or do you secretly attribute effects to your own power or to natural causes as if they were independent?
?
The cause can be prior in essence while coexisting in time. Does your worship reflect that God is prior to you in essence—that He owes you nothing—or do you expect a reciprocal relationship as if you and He were two temporal beings interacting on equal footing?
