Types of Infinite Regress: Possible and Impossible
Classifies four types of infinite sequences and their possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of infinite sequences and why only those with intrinsic ordering (positional or natural) are impossible, while successive events and human souls can be infinite.

Core Idea
Not all infinite regresses are impossible; only those with essential arrangement.

The author responds to an objection: if an actually infinite body is impossible, why aren't infinite successive motions also impossible? He clarifies by classifying infinite regress into four types: two that require a first term (bodies with spatial parts, causal chains) and two that do not (events in time, human souls). This distinction is crucial for arguments about the eternity of the world and the soul.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Read the opening 'If it is said...' to understand the challenge: why is an infinite body impossible but an infinite series of events not also impossible? This sets up the need for classification.
The objection assumes that all infinite regresses are alike.
2
Identify the two impossible types
Find the two divisions that cannot be infinite: bodies (positional arrangement) and causes/effects (natural arrangement). Notice the reasoning: both have parts or items with essential relations to each other, requiring a first.
Positional arrangement involves spatial contiguity; natural arrangement involves causal dependence.
3
Learn the two possible types
Find the two divisions that can be infinite: successive events/motions (no essential order) and human souls. Note the condition: they lack intrinsic ordering, so they can succeed each other indefinitely.
The passage cuts off for souls, but the classification is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: قد دل البرهان العقلي على أن جسماً ما لا تتناهى ذاته بالفعل مستحيل وجوده، فبينوا أن حركات متعاقبة وحوادث متتالية لا تتناهى مستحيل وجودها؛ فإن عندنا التناهي واللاتناهي إنما يتطرق إلى أربعة أقسام؛ اثنين منها لا يجوز أن يوجدا غير متناهيين الذات، وهو ما له ترتيب وضعي كالجسم، أو طبيعي كالعلل، فجسم لا تتناهى ذاته مستحيل وجوده، وعلل ومعلولات لا تتناهى بالعدد مستحيل وجودها أيضا، ولكل واحد من القسمين ترتيب في الوجود؛ أما الجسم فله ترتيب وضعي ولأجزائه اتصال ولكل جزء منه إلى جزء نسبة فلا يجوز وجود جسم غير متناه في زمان واحد، وأما العلل فلها ترتيب طبيعي، فإن المعلول يتعلق بعلته، ولكل معلول إلى علته نسبة، فلا يجوز وجود جسم وعلل ومعلولات لا تتناهى. وأما القسمان اللذان يوجدان غير متناهيي الذات، فإن أحدهما الحوادث والحركات التي لا تترتب بعضها على بعض لضرورة ذاتها، بل تتعاقب وتتوالى شيئًا بعد شيء في أزمنة غير متناهية، فإن ذلك جايز عقلا، والثاني النفوس الإنسانية فإنها
If it is said: Rational demonstration has proven that a body whose essence is actually infinite is impossible in existence. So explain that successive motions and consecutive events that are infinite are impossible in existence. For with us, finitude and infinitude pertain to four divisions. Two of them are not permissible to exist as infinite in essence: that which has positional arrangement (tartīb waḍʿī) like a body, or natural arrangement (tartīb ṭabīʿī) like causes. So a body whose essence is infinite is impossible in existence, and causes and effects infinite in number are also impossible in existence. And each of the two divisions has arrangement in existence. As for the body, it has positional arrangement and its parts have connection, and each part has a relation to (another) part, so it is not permissible for a body to exist infinite in one time. As for causes, they have natural arrangement, for the effect is dependent on its cause, and each effect has a relation to its cause, so it is not permissible for a body, causes, and effects to be infinite. As for the two divisions that exist as infinite in essence: one of them is events (al-ḥawādith) and motions that are not arranged upon each other by necessity of their essence, but rather succeed and follow one after another in infinite times, for that is rationally possible. And the second is human souls (al-nufūs al-insāniyya), for they...
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of four types of infinite regress. The author addresses an objection: if an actually infinite body is impossible, then an infinite series of events is also impossible. He responds by distinguishing two categories that cannot be infinite: (1) things with positional arrangement (tartīb waḍʿī), like a body whose parts are arranged in space and have a relation of contiguity; (2) things with natural arrangement (tartīb ṭabīʿī), like a chain of causes and effects where each effect depends on its cause. Both require a first term and cannot be actually infinite. The two categories that can be infinite are: (1) events and motions that occur successively without an intrinsic ordering—they merely succeed one another in time and can be infinite; (2) human souls. The explanation for souls is cut off, but the classification is clear. The key point is that an infinite regress is impossible only when the items have a relational ordering that presupposes a first; without such ordering, an infinite series is rationally possible (jāʾiz ʿaqlan).

Key Terms
ترتيب وضعي
ترتيب طبيعي
علل
معلولات
حوادث
نفوس إنسانية
لاتناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All infinite regresses are impossible.
Correction: Only those with an intrinsic ordering (like spatial parts or causal chains) are impossible; successive events without essential order can be infinite.

Checkpoint
Which of the following can be infinite according to this classification?
A) A chain of causes and effects.
B) The parts of a body.
C) Successive motions in time.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress is impossible only when the items have an intrinsic relational ordering that presupposes a first term; otherwise, it is rationally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes human souls as potentially infinite in number. Your soul is one of them. Does this make you feel insignificant or part of a grand eternal plan? How does this truth affect the way you view your own existence before God?
?
If events in your life have no essential ordering (just one after another), does that mean they are meaningless? Or can you still see God's wisdom in their sequence? What would change in your attitude if you believed every event is essentially ordered by God?
?
The distinction between impossible infinite regress (causes) and possible infinite regress (events) rests on whether there is a natural or positional arrangement. Which type of arrangement describes your relationship with God: are you merely 'positioned' as a creature, or are you essentially dependent on Him as a 'natural' effect? What does that demand from you today?

The Objector's Classification of Infinite Multiplicities
Objection classifying four types of infinite and their permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's classification of infinite entities into four categories based on presence or absence of spatial or causal order.

Core Idea
The objector claims ordered infinites are impossible, but unordered ones like events and souls can be infinite.

Before the author presents his universal refutation, an objector raises a sophisticated challenge: perhaps not all infinites are impossible. He distinguishes four kinds of infinite entities—some with spatial order (bodies), some with causal order (chains), some successive in time (events), and some coexisting without order (souls). Only the first two are deemed impossible. This classification sets the stage for the author's sweeping proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's classification
Carefully read the Arabic passage from 'فإن قيل' to 'فيجوز أن توجد غير متناهية'. Note the four categories presented.
The objector concedes rational proof shows infinite bodies impossible, but questions why infinite successive events should be impossible.
2
Identify the two impossible categories
The objector says two categories cannot exist as infinite in essence: those with spatial order (body) and those with natural order (causal chains). Understand why each has an inherent order.
Spatial order: parts have contiguity and ratio. Natural order: effect depends on cause.
3
Identify the two possible categories
The objector says two categories can exist as infinite: successive events/motions (temporal sequence without essential order) and human souls (coexisting without spatial or causal order).
These lack the kind of order that would force finitude according to the objector.
4
Prepare for the author's answer
Notice that the objector's argument rests on the type of order. The author will challenge the very notion that any infinite multiplicity is conceivable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد دل البرهان العقلي على أن جسماً ما لا تتناهى ذاته بالفعل مستحيل وجوده، فبينوا أن حركات متعاقبة وحوادث متتالية لا تتناهى مستحيل وجودها؛ فإن عندنا التناهي واللاتناهي إنما يتطرق إلى أربعة أقسام؛ اثنين منها لا يجوز أن يوجدا غير متناهيين الذات، وهو ما له ترتيب وضعي كالجسم، أو طبيعي كالعلل، فجسم لا تتناهى ذاته مستحيل وجوده، وعلل ومعلولات لا تتناهى بالعدد مستحيل وجودها أيضا، ولكل واحد من القسمين ترتيب في الوجود؛ أما الجسم فله ترتيب وضعي ولأجزائه اتصال ولكل جزء منه إلى جزء نسبة فلا يجوز وجود جسم غير متناه في زمان واحد، وأما العلل فلها ترتيب طبيعي، فإن المعلول يتعلق بعلته، ولكل معلول إلى علته نسبة، فلا يجوز وجود جسم وعلل ومعلولات لا تتناهى. وأما القسمان اللذان يوجدان غير متناهيي الذات، فإن أحدهما الحوادث والحركات التي لا تترتب بعضها على بعض لضرورة ذاتها، بل تتعاقب وتتوالى شيئًا بعد شيء في أزمنة غير متناهية، فإن ذلك جائز عقلا، والثاني النفوس الإنسانية فإنها موجودات لم تتعاقب، بل هي مجتمعة في الوجود ولكن لا ترتيب لها وضعا كالجسم، ولا طبعا كالعلل، فيجوز أن توجد غير متناهية. والجواب : قلنا : كلما حصره الوجود وكان قابلا للنهاية فهو متناءٍ ضرورة، وما لا يتناهى لا يتصور وجوده سواء كان له ترتيب وضعي أو طبيعي أو لم يكن. والبرهان على ذلك بحيث يعم الأقسام الأربعة أن كل كثرة لا تتناهى فلا تخلو؛ إما أن تكون غير متناهية من وجه، أو غير متناهية من جميع الوجوه والجهات، وعلى القسمين جميعا فإنا يمكننا أن نفصل منهما جزءا بالوهم أو بالعقل، فنأخذ تلك الكثرة مع ذلك الجزء شيئًا على حدة، ونأخذها بانفرادها شيئًا على حدة فلا يخلو إما أن تكون تلك الكثرة مع تلك الزيادة مساوية للكثرة من غير الزيادة عددًا أو مساحة فيلزم أن يكون الزائد مثل الناقص ومساويًا له، وهذا محال، وإما أن لا يكون مساويًا له فيلزم أن يكون كثرتان غير متناهيتين وإحداهما أكبر والثانية أنقص، وهو أيضا محال. وهذا البرهان إن فرضته في جسم غير متناءٍ من جميع الجهات أو من جهة واحدة فالعبارة عنه أن نقول لك أن تفرض نقطة من ذلك الجسم، ويفصل منه مقدارا، فتأخذ ذلك الجسم مع ذلك المقدار شيئًا على حدة، وبانفراده شيئًا على حدة، ثم نطبق بين النقطتين المتناهيتين في الوهم فلا يخلو؛ إما أن يكونا بحيث يمتدان معا مطابقين في الامتداد إلى ما لا يتناهى فيكون الزائد والناقص متساويين، وإما أن لا
For it is said: The rational proof has indicated that a body whose essence is actually infinite is impossible to exist, so explain that successive motions and consecutive events that are infinite are impossible to exist. For with us, finitude and infinitude pertain only to four categories: two of which it is not permissible for them to exist as infinite in essence—those having spatial order like a body, or natural order like causes. So a body whose essence is infinite is impossible to exist, and causes and effects infinite in number are also impossible to exist. Each of the two categories has order in existence: as for body, it has spatial order and its parts have contiguity, and each part has a ratio to another part, so it is not permissible for a body infinite in extent to exist in a single time. As for causes, they have natural order, for the effect depends on its cause, and each effect has a ratio to its cause, so it is not permissible for a body and causes and effects to exist as infinite. As for the two categories that exist as infinite in essence: one of them is events and motions that are not ordered in relation to each other by necessity of their essence, but rather they follow and succeed one after another in infinite times, and that is rationally permissible. The second is human souls, for they are existing things that do not follow each other, but are gathered in existence, having neither spatial order like a body nor natural order like causes, so it is permissible for them to exist as infinite. The answer: We say: Everything that existence encompasses and is receptive of finitude is necessarily finite, and what is infinite cannot be conceived to exist whether it has spatial or natural order or not. The proof of this, such that it encompasses the four categories, is that every multiplicity that is infinite must be either infinite in one respect or infinite in all respects and directions. In both cases, we can separate from it a part by imagination or intellect, then take that multiplicity with that part as one thing, and take it alone as another thing. Then either that multiplicity with the addition is equal to the multiplicity without addition in number or magnitude, so that the additional is like the deficient and equal to it—and this is impossible—or it is not equal to it, so that there are two infinite multiplicities, one larger and the other smaller—and this is also impossible. This proof, if you apply it to a body infinite from all directions or from one direction, the expression for it is to say: you assume a point from that body, and you separate a magnitude from it, then you take that body with that magnitude as one thing, and by itself as another thing, then you apply between the two finite points in imagination. Then either they are such that they extend together matching in extension to infinity, so that the additional and the deficient are equal, or they are not.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objection begins by conceding that rational proof has shown that a body infinite in itself is impossible. But it questions why then successive motions and events that are infinite are also impossible. The objector classifies infinitude into four categories. Two are impossible: bodies (spatial order) and causal chains (natural order). Two are possible: successive events (temporal sequence without essential order) and human souls (coexisting without order). The answer refutes this: any multiplicity that exists and is receptive to finitude is necessarily finite. The proof applies to all four categories: for any infinite multiplicity, we can separate a part and compare the whole with the part. If the whole with the part equals the whole without the part, then the part is equal to the whole—impossible. If not, then we have two infinities where one is larger—also impossible. This reduces the objection's classification to absurdity. The proof is then illustrated using the example of an infinite body.

Key Terms
ترتيب وضعي
ترتيب طبيعي
لا يتناهى
تجويز عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any infinite multiplicity is impossible, but the objector argues that only ordered infinites are impossible.
Correction: The passage shows that the objector distinguishes between ordered and unordered infinites, claiming that unordered ones (events, souls) can be infinite.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, which of the following categories can be infinite in essence?
A) A body with spatial order
B) A causal chain of causes and effects
C) Successive events without essential order
D) Only bodies and causes

Key Takeaway
The objector classifies infinities by presence of order: ordered are impossible, unordered are possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that the objector accepts infinite souls because they lack order. But your own soul—do you experience it as a mere unordered entity among countless others, or do you sense a divine ordering that gives it purpose? How does your response change the way you live today?
?
The objector says successive events can be infinite because they have no essential order. Yet every event in your life is under Allah's decree. If you truly believed that, would you treat your past mistakes as mere unordered accidents, or as part of a meaningful chain guiding you to Him? What step can you take now to embrace that order?
?
Bodies and causes, the objector claims, cannot be infinite because of their order. But you are a finite being with a body and causes around you. Does this reminder of your finitude humble you, or do you still act as though you control limitless horizons? What one attachment to this finite world must you release today to prepare for the infinite?

The Universal Proof Against Any Infinite Multiplicity
Universal proof that any infinite multiplicity leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's universal proof that any infinite multiplicity leads to logical contradiction, regardless of order.

Core Idea
Comparing a part with its whole in any infinite multiplicity leads to absurdity.

Having presented the objection that classifies infinite multiplicities into possible and impossible based on order, the author now responds with a universal proof that encompasses all four categories. The proof shows that any infinite multiplicity, whether ordered or not, leads to a contradiction when we compare a part with the whole. This is a powerful refutation that cuts across the objector's distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's initial refutation statement
Begin at 'والجواب' and read the first sentence: 'كلما حصره الوجود...'. This is the summary of the answer.
The author declares that anything existent and receptive to finitude is necessarily finite.
2
Understand the universal proof structure
Read from 'والبرهان على ذلك' to the end. The proof considers any infinite multiplicity, separates a part by imagination, and compares the whole with and without that part.
Two cases: either the whole with the part equals the whole without (so part=whole) or they are unequal (so two infinities of different sizes). Both impossible.
3
Apply the proof to a body example
The author then applies the proof to a body infinite from all directions. The text becomes cut off, but the idea is the same: comparing a finite segment with the infinite body leads to contradiction.
The proof is incomplete in the source; the full conclusion would be on the next page.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Notice how this proof does not depend on the type of order. It works for bodies, causes, events, and souls alike. This demolishes the objector's classification.

This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objection begins by conceding that rational proof has shown that a body infinite in itself is impossible. The General Proof Against Any Infinite Multitude
Presentation of the objection and general proof against infinite multitudes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general rational proof that demonstrates the impossibility of an infinite multitude, covering both ordered and unordered beings.

Core Idea
Any supposed infinite multitude leads to a logical contradiction.

In this passage, al-Shahrastani addresses the claim that some multitudes—especially those without order—could be infinite. He presents a universal proof that shows any supposed infinite multitude, whether ordered or not, results in an absurdity. This proof is foundational for arguments about the finitude of the world and its components.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the objection that unordered beings might be infinite. Then read al-Shahrastani's initial answer that whatever is encompassed by existence and has an end is finite.
The objection is the first sentence; the answer begins with 'والجواب'.
2
Understand the two cases of infinity
The proof divides an infinite multitude into two cases: infinite from one aspect or infinite from all aspects. Recognize that both cases are considered.
The text says 'إما أن تكون غير متناهية من وجه، أو غير متناهية من جميع الوجوه'.
3
Grasp the reductio argument
The proof isolates a part from the whole and compares. Either the whole with the part equals the whole without the part (absurd), or they are unequal (leading to two infinites of different sizes, also absurd). This is the core logical contradiction.
The argument uses imagination (وهم) or reason to separate a part.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that the only way to avoid absurdity is to deny the possibility of any infinite multitude. This refutes the objection.
The proof is general and covers all types of multitudes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موجودات لم تتعاقب، بل هي مجتمعة في الوجود ولكن لا ترتيب لها وضعا كالجسم، ولا طبعا كالعلل، فيجوز أن توجد غير متناهية. والجواب : قلنا : كلما حصره الوجود وكان قابلا للنهاية فهو متناءٍ ضرورة، وما لا يتناهى لا يتصور وجوده سواء كان له ترتيب وضعي أو طبيعي أو لم يكن. والبرهان على ذلك بحيث يعم الأقسام الأربعة أن كل كثرة لا تتناهى فلا تخلو إما أن تكون غير متناهية من وجه، أو غير متناهية من جميع الوجوه والجهات، وعلى القسمين جميعا فإنا يمكننا أن نفصل منهما جزءا بالوهم أو بالعقل، فنأخذ تلك الكثرة مع ذلك الجزء شيئًا على حدة، ونأخذها بانفرادها شيئًا على حدة فلا يخلو إما أن تكون تلك الكثرة مع تلك الزيادة مساوية للكثرة من غير الزيادة عددًا أو مساحة فيلزم أن يكون الزائد مثل الناقص ومساويًا له، وهذا محال، وإما أن لا يكون مساويًا له فيلزم أن يكون كثرتان غير متناهيتين وإحداهما أكبر والثانية أنقص، وهو أيضا محال. وهذا البرهان إن فرضته في جسم غير متناءٍ من جميع الجهات أو من جهة واحدة فالعبارة عنه أن نقول لك أن تفرض نقطة من ذلك الجسم، ويفصل منه مقدارا، فتأخذ ذلك الجسم مع ذلك المقدار شيئًا على حدة، وبانفراده شيئًا على حدة، ثم نطبق بين النقطتين المتناهيتين في الوهم فلا يخلو إما أن يكونا بحيث يمتدان معا مطابقين في الامتداد إلى ما لا يتناهى فيكون الزائد والناقص متساويين، وإما أن لا يمتد بل يقصر أحدهما عنه فيكون متناهيا والقدر المفضول كان متناهيا أيضا، فيكون المجموع متناهيًا أيضا وقد فرضه غير متناه هذا خلف، وكذلك إن فرضته في حركات غير متناهية، أو أشخاص غير متناهية متعاقبة أو مجتمعة، أو تفرض نفوسا غير متناهية معا أو مجتمعة في الوجود، فالعبارة عنه أن نقول لك أن تفرض حركة واحدة أو شخصاً واحدًا، وتفصل من الحركات أو الأشخاص مقدارا بالتوهم، وتنسب الحركات الزائدة إلى الحركات الناقصة، وتم البرهان من غير تفاوت بين الصورتين.
Beings that are not successive but are together in existence, having no positional order like a body, nor natural order like causes, so it is permissible that they exist infinitely. The answer: We said: Everything that existence encompasses and is capable of an end is necessarily finite, and that which is infinite cannot be conceived to exist, whether it has positional or natural order or not. The proof of this, covering the four divisions, is that every infinite multitude is either infinite from one aspect or infinite from all aspects and directions. In both cases, we can separate a part from it by imagination or by reason, and take that multitude with that part as a single thing, and take it alone as a single thing. Then it is either that that multitude with that addition is equal to the multitude without the addition in number or magnitude, so it follows that the increase is like the decrease and equal to it—this is absurd—or it is not equal to it, so it follows that there are two infinite multitudes, one greater and the other lesser, which is also absurd. This proof, if you suppose it in a body infinite from all sides or from one side, the statement about it is that you suppose a point in that body and separate from it a magnitude, then take that body with that magnitude as a single thing and by itself as a single thing, then match between the two finite points in the imagination. Then either they both extend together, coinciding in extension to infinity, so that the increase and the decrease are equal, or they do not extend but one falls short, so it is finite, and the deficient magnitude is also finite, so the total is also finite, and it was supposed infinite—this is a contradiction. Likewise, if you suppose it in infinite motions, or infinite individuals successive or together, or suppose infinite souls together or coexisting in existence, the statement about it is that you suppose one motion or one individual, and separate from the motions or individuals a quantity by imagination, and relate the additional motions to the deficient motions, and the proof is completed without difference between the two cases.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection: some might claim that beings that are not successive, such as bodies coexisting without any natural or positional order, could be infinite. The author refutes this with a general proof that any infinite multitude is impossible. The proof considers two cases: (1) infinity from one aspect only, and (2) infinity from all aspects. In both, one can mentally isolate a part and compare the original whole with it. Two absurd consequences follow: either the whole with the part equals the whole without it (the part equals the whole, impossible), or there are two infinite multitudes one greater than the other (also impossible). The author then applies the same reasoning to a body infinite in extent: subtracting a finite part yields a contradiction. He extends it to infinite motions, individuals, and souls, whether successive or simultaneous, concluding that the proof works identically for all, with no essential difference.

Key Terms
كثرة لا تتناهى (infinite multitude)

Correction: The proof shows that any infinite multitude, regardless of order, leads to a logical contradiction.

Any infinite multitude leads to a logical contradiction, so infinite existence is impossible.

?
The proof uses the impossibility of part being equal to whole. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes treat small sins as if they are equivalent to the whole of good deeds? How will you realign your perspective to see each sin as a real deficit that needs repentance, not something to be ignored?

Applications of the Proof to Bodies, Motions, and Souls
Application of the general proof to specific types of infinite multitudes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the general proof against infinite multitudes applies to bodies, motions, successive individuals, and coexisting souls.

Core Idea
The same logical proof works for all types of infinite multitudes.

After establishing the general proof, al-Shahrastani applies it to specific cases: a body infinite in extent, infinite motions, infinite individuals (successive or together), and infinite souls coexisting. He shows that in each case, subtracting a finite part leads to the same two absurdities, confirming the universality of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the application to a body
Read the passage where the proof is applied to a body that is infinite from all sides or one side. Notice how you suppose a point, separate a magnitude, and compare.
The text says 'إن فرضته في جسم غير متناءٍ من جميع الجهات أو من جهة واحدة'.
2
Understand the mapping argument
The proof maps the two finite points in imagination. Either they extend together coinciding to infinity (making increase and decrease equal, absurd) or one falls short (making both finite, contradiction).
The mapping (تطبيق) is a conceptual co-extension.
3
Extend to motions and individuals
Read how the same reasoning applies to infinite motions or infinite individuals, whether successive or simultaneous. You suppose one motion or one individual, separate a quantity, and compare.
The proof is identical in structure.
4
Apply to coexisting souls
Finally, consider souls that are together in existence. The proof works the same way: you suppose one soul, separate a number, and compare.
The author says there is no difference between the cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موجودات لم تتعاقب، بل هي مجتمعة في الوجود ولكن لا ترتيب لها وضعا كالجسم، ولا طبعا كالعلل، فيجوز أن توجد غير متناهية. والجواب : قلنا : كلما حصره الوجود وكان قابلا للنهاية فهو متناءٍ ضرورة، وما لا يتناهى لا يتصور وجوده سواء كان له ترتيب وضعي أو طبيعي أو لم يكن. والبرهان على ذلك بحيث يعم الأقسام الأربعة أن كل كثرة لا تتناهى فلا تخلو إما أن تكون غير متناهية من وجه، أو غير متناهية من جميع الوجوه والجهات، وعلى القسمين جميعا فإنا يمكننا أن نفصل منهما جزءا بالوهم أو بالعقل، فنأخذ تلك الكثرة مع ذلك الجزء شيئًا على حدة، ونأخذها بانفرادها شيئًا على حدة فلا يخلو إما أن تكون تلك الكثرة مع تلك الزيادة مساوية للكثرة من غير الزيادة عددًا أو مساحة فيلزم أن يكون الزائد مثل الناقص ومساويًا له، وهذا محال، وإما أن لا يكون مساويًا له فيلزم أن يكون كثرتان غير متناهيتين وإحداهما أكبر والثانية أنقص، وهو أيضا محال. وهذا البرهان إن فرضته في جسم غير متناءٍ من جميع الجهات أو من جهة واحدة فالعبارة عنه أن نقول لك أن تفرض نقطة من ذلك الجسم، ويفصل منه مقدارا، فتأخذ ذلك الجسم مع ذلك المقدار شيئًا على حدة، وبانفراده شيئًا على حدة، ثم نطبق بين النقطتين المتناهيتين في الوهم فلا يخلو إما أن يكونا بحيث يمتدان معا مطابقين في الامتداد إلى ما لا يتناهى فيكون الزائد والناقص متساويين، وإما أن لا يمتد بل يقصر أحدهما عنه فيكون متناهيا والقدر المفضول كان متناهيا أيضا، فيكون المجموع متناهيًا أيضا وقد فرضه غير متناه هذا خلف، وكذلك إن فرضته في حركات غير متناهية، أو أشخاص غير متناهية متعاقبة أو مجتمعة، أو تفرض نفوسا غير متناهية معا أو مجتمعة في الوجود، فالعبارة عنه أن نقول لك أن تفرض حركة واحدة أو شخصاً واحدًا، وتفصل من الحركات أو الأشخاص مقدارا بالتوهم، وتنسب الحركات الزائدة إلى الحركات الناقصة، وتم البرهان من غير تفاوت بين الصورتين.
Beings that are not successive but are together in existence, having no positional order like a body, nor natural order like causes, so it is permissible that they exist infinitely. The answer: We said: Everything that existence encompasses and is capable of an end is necessarily finite, and that which is infinite cannot be conceived to exist, whether it has positional or natural order or not. The proof of this, covering the four divisions, is that every infinite multitude is either infinite from one aspect or infinite from all aspects and directions. In both cases, we can separate a part from it by imagination or by reason, and take that multitude with that part as a single thing, and take it alone as a single thing. Then it is either that that multitude with that addition is equal to the multitude without the addition in number or magnitude, so it follows that the increase is like the decrease and equal to it—this is absurd—or it is not equal to it, so it follows that there are two infinite multitudes, one greater and the other lesser, which is also absurd. This proof, if you suppose it in a body infinite from all sides or from one side, the statement about it is that you suppose a point in that body and separate from it a magnitude, then take that body with that magnitude as a single thing and by itself as a single thing, then match between the two finite points in the imagination. Then either they both extend together, coinciding in extension to infinity, so that the increase and the decrease are equal, or they do not extend but one falls short, so it is finite, and the deficient magnitude is also finite, so the total is also finite, and it was supposed infinite—this is a contradiction. Likewise, if you suppose it in infinite motions, or infinite individuals successive or together, or suppose infinite souls together or coexisting in existence, the statement about it is that you suppose one motion or one individual, and separate from the motions or individuals a quantity by imagination, and relate the additional motions to the deficient motions, and the proof is completed without difference between the two cases.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection: some might claim that beings that are not successive, such as bodies coexisting without any natural or positional order, could be infinite. The author refutes this with a general proof that any infinite multitude is impossible. The proof considers two cases: (1) infinity from one aspect only, and (2) infinity from all aspects. In both, one can mentally isolate a part and compare the original whole with it. Two absurd consequences follow: either the whole with the part equals the whole without it (the part equals the whole, impossible), or there are two infinite multitudes one greater than the other (also impossible). The author then applies the same reasoning to a body infinite in extent: subtracting a finite part yields a contradiction. He extends it to infinite motions, individuals, and souls, whether successive or simultaneous, concluding that the proof works identically for all, with no essential difference.

جسم (body)
حركات (motions)
أشخاص (individuals)
نفوس (souls)
تطبيق (mapping)
خلف (contradiction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof only works for objects with spatial order.
Correction: The author explicitly applies it to non-spatial entities like motions and souls, showing it is universal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the result when you map the two finite points in the body example?
A) Either they extend together coinciding to infinity (making increase and decrease equal) or one falls short (making the total finite).
B) The points always coincide, proving the body is finite.
C) The points are incommensurable, so no conclusion is reached.
D) The body must be infinite to cover both points.

Key Takeaway
The general proof against infinite multitudes applies uniformly to bodies, motions, individuals, and souls, confirming its universality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If no created thing—not even your sufferings or joys—can be infinite, then your trials are finite and your blessings are finite. Does that change how you endure hardship or how you cherish moments of ease? Will you begin to see each trial as a limited assignment from the Infinite, not an endless burden?
?
The same proof applies to souls: there cannot be an infinite number of souls. Your soul is one of a finite number, each uniquely created by the Infinite. How does this compel you to treat your own soul as precious and accountable, and to honor the souls of others as equally finite and destined to meet the Infinite?
?
You may act as though your time, your worries, or your ambitions are infinite. The proof shows that no multitude—including your days and your thoughts—can be without limit. Ibn Sina's Objection and the Answer: Positional Order vs. Succession
Ibn Sina distinguishes spatial from temporal series and the refutation of that distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's objection that successive motions lack positional order for the mapping argument, and the first part of the answer that uses imagination to treat them as simultaneous.

Core Idea
Motions lack spatial order, but imagination can map them temporally.

This lesson introduces a critical objection from Ibn Sina against the proof of infinite motions. He argues that the proof only works for bodies with positional order, not for successive motions. The answer begins by showing that the entire argument is conducted in the imagination, so one can imagine motions as if they coexist on an infinite temporal line.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where Ibn Sina presents his distinction between bodies (positional order) and motions (no positional order).
Notice the key term 'ترتيب وضعي' (positional order).
2
Identify the distinction
Understand why Ibn Sina says the mapping argument fails for motions: they are not simultaneous, so you cannot determine a point to base the mapping.
3
Read the answer's first aspect
Read the response starting with 'قيل له'. The first aspect says everything is imagined, so you can imagine past motions as if they exist simultaneously on an imaginary temporal line.
The answer has two aspects; only the first is fully present here.
4
Understand the role of imagination
The answer explains that the entire proof is in imagination (بالوهم), so the lack of real simultaneity is irrelevant; you can mentally treat motions like points on a line.
This preserves the validity of the mapping argument.
5
Note the missing second aspect
The passage ends abruptly; the second aspect (regarding the cause of impossibility) is not fully given but is known from context.
The full answer would reinforce that the proof works for any series.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو علي بن سينا هاهنا فرق، وهو أن الجسم له ترتيب وضعي أمكنك أن تعين فيه نقطة ثم ترتب عليها جواز الانطباق والامتداد إلى ما لا يتناهى، وليس للحركات المتتالية ترتيب وضعي فإنها ليست معا في زمان واحد، فلا يمكنك أن تعين فيها حركة واحدة وترتب عليها جواز الانطباق؛ لأن ما لا يترتب في الوضع أو الطبع فلا يحتمل الانطباق. قيل له : جوابك عن هذا الفرق من وجهين: أحدهما : أنك فرضت النقطة في الجسم والامتداد إلى ما لا يتناهى بالوهم، وقدرت التطبيق أيضا بالوهم، فقدر وهما أن الحركات الماضية كأنها موجودة متعاقبة، والأزمنة أيضا كأنها موجودة وهما متعاقبة على مثال خط موهوم لا يتناهى تركب من نقط متتالية والحدود في الحركات والأوقات في الأزمنة كالنقط في الخطوط والتعاقب كالتالي، والعلة التي لأجلها امتنع اللاتتناهي، ووجب أن تتناهى فهو أنه يؤدي إلى أنه يكون الأقل مثل الأكثر، والناقص مثل الزائد، وهذا
Abu Ali ibn Sina said: There is a difference here: body has a positional order such that you can determine a point in it and then base upon it the possibility of mapping and extension to infinity. Successive motions do not have positional order, for they are not together in a single time, so you cannot determine a single motion in them and base upon it the possibility of mapping, because that which is not arranged in position or nature does not admit mapping. It was said to him: The answer to this difference is from two aspects: First: you supposed the point in the body and extension to infinity by imagination, and you estimated the mapping also by imagination. So imagine that the past motions are as if they exist successively, and the times are also as if they exist, both successively, on the model of an imaginary infinite line composed of successive points, and the boundaries in motions and times are like points in lines, and succession like following. And the cause on account of which infinitude is impossible and finitude necessary is that it leads to the less being like the greater, and the deficient like the excess. And this...
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Ibn Sina against the preceding proof. He argues that the proof works for bodies because they have spatial (positional) order, allowing point-to-point mapping, but successive motions have no such order, as they occur one after another. Therefore the mapping argument cannot be applied to motions. The answer, given in two parts, first replies that everything in the proof is done in the imagination (بالوهم), so one can imagine past motions as if they existed simultaneously on an imaginary temporal line, with temporal points functioning like spatial points, thus the same mapping applies. The second part (incomplete in this window) states that the underlying reason for impossibility is the absurdity of the less equaling the greater, which holds universally. Thus the proof remains valid for motions as well.

Key Terms
فرق (distinction)
ترتيب وضعي (positional order)
انطباق (mapping/coincidence)
وهم (imagination)
تعاقب (succession)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof only works for physical bodies because they have spatial order.
Correction: The answer shows that the proof works for any series, including motions, via mental representation in imagination, not requiring actual spatial coexistence.

Checkpoint
What is Ibn Sina's main objection to the proof regarding infinite motions?
A) Motions cannot be infinite because they are temporal.
B) Motions lack positional order needed for the mapping argument.
C) The proof only applies to souls, not motions.
D) The proof requires actual physical mapping, which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's objection is answered by showing that the mapping argument works in imagination for any series, including successive motions.

Ibn Sina's Distinction on Infinity
Ibn Sina distinguishes bodies from motions regarding infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's key distinction between bodies and successive motions, and why it blocks the application of the infinity proof to motion series.

Core Idea
Bodies have positional order; motions do not—so the proof fails for motions.

Ibn Sina intervenes with a subtle but decisive objection. He argues that the proof of infinite regression only applies to things that can be simultaneously arranged, like bodies. Successive motions, however, occur one after another in time, so they cannot be mapped onto each other. This distinction is crucial for separating arguments about physical bodies from arguments about temporal sequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ibn Sina's own wording come through before you interpret.
Notice the key phrase 'ترتيب وضعي' (positional order).
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two cases: bodies and successive motions. Find how Ibn Sina contrasts them regarding positional order and the possibility of applying the coincidence argument.
3
Understand the logical implication
Ask yourself: why does the lack of positional order in motions make the infinity proof inapplicable? Connect this to the idea that motions are not simultaneous.
This clarifies the scope of the earlier proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو علي بن سينا هاهنا فرق، وهو أن الجسم له ترتيب وضعي أمكنك أن تعين فيه نقطة ثم ترتب عليها جواز الانطباق والامتداد إلى ما لا يتناهى، وليس للحركات المتتالية ترتيب وضعي فإنها ليست معا في زمان واحد، فلا يمكنك أن تعين فيها حركة واحدة وترتب عليها جواز الانطباق ؛ لأن ما لا يترتب في الوضع أو الطبع فلا يحتمل الانطباق.
Abu Ali ibn Sina said here a distinction: a body has a positional order such that you can specify a point in it and then infer upon it the possibility of coincidence and extension ad infinitum, but successive motions do not have a positional order because they are not together in one time, so you cannot specify one motion in them and infer upon it the possibility of coincidence; for that which does not have order in position or nature does not admit coincidence.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's objection to the preceding proof. He argues that the proof only works for objects that have a positional order (ترتيب وضعي), like bodies, where you can identify a point and extend it infinitely. Successive motions, however, are not simultaneous in time, so they lack such order. Therefore, the argument of applying (انطباق) one part onto another does not apply to motions. This distinction aims to block the application of the infinity proof to temporal series.

Key Terms
ترتيب وضعي (positional order)
انطباق (coincidence/applicability)
حركات متتالية (successive motions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ibn Sina is denying the impossibility of infinite motion series entirely.
Correction: Ibn Sina is not denying the impossibility; he is only denying that the particular proof of infinite regression applies to motions, because they lack the required positional order.

Checkpoint

A) Because motions are not real.
B) Because motions lack a positional order that allows coincidence.
C) Because motions are finite by nature.
D) Because the proof only works for souls.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's distinction shows that the nature of the items in a series—whether they have positional order or not—determines whether certain infinity proofs apply.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In your own reasoning about God and creation, have you ever assumed that what is true for physical objects is automatically true for events or actions? The positions of the heart and soul are not like the positions of a body. How does this distinction affect how you think about spiritual progress and accountability before Allah?

Juwayni's Argument Against Infinite Time
Juwayni presents a reductio argument against actual infinite temporal series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's argument that an infinite past and future leads to contradiction, using the present day as a starting point.

Core Idea
An actual infinite series leads to absurdity when a finite portion is transferred.

This lesson presents a classic kalam argument for the finitude of time. The mutakallim invites us to consider the present day. He shows that if both past and future were infinite, moving a finite segment from past to future would make them unequal, contradicting their equal infinity. This argument establishes that time cannot be actually infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a conditional supposition and a logical operation.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two infinite sets
The argument assumes both past and future are infinite. Identify how the text describes their equality.
The phrase 'متماثلان في نفي التناهي' is key.
3
Understand the operation
The mutakallim proposes taking a finite portion from the past and adding it to the future. Visualize what happens to each set.
This is a thought experiment.
4
Recognize the contradiction
After the transfer, the past becomes deficient and the future becomes surplus, yet they were both infinite and equal. This leads to the absurd conclusion that the surplus equals the deficient.
This is the crux of the reductio.
5
Reflect on the implication
If the assumption of an actual infinite leads to contradiction, then time must have a beginning and an end. Consider why this matters for the existence of God.
This argument is used to prove that the world is temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المتكلم: إنا إذا فرضنا الكلام في اليوم الذي نحن فيه، وقلنا ما مضى من الأيام غير متناه عندكم، وما بقي أيضا غير متناه، والباقي والماضي متساويان متماثلان في عدم التناهي، فلو فصلنا من الماضي وأضفناها إلى الباقي صار الماضي ناقصاً والباقي زايدًا، وهما متماثلان في نفي التناهي، أدى ذلك إلى أن يكون الزايد مثل الناقص.
The mutakallim said: If we suppose the speech concerning the day we are in, and we say that the days that have passed are infinite according to you, and what remains is also infinite, and the remaining and the past are equal and similar in being infinite, then if we separate from the past and add it to the remaining, the past becomes deficient and the remaining becomes surplus, while they are similar in the negation of infinity, that leads to the surplus being like the deficient.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) against the possibility of an actual infinite number of past events. The mutakallim presents a reductio ad absurdum. He asks us to consider the present day. According to those who claim an infinite past, the days that have passed are infinite, and the days remaining are also infinite. He notes that both the past and the future are equal in being infinite (lā mutanāhī). If we take a finite number of days from the past and add them to the future, the past becomes deficient (naqisa) and the future becomes increased (za'id). But since they were both infinite and equal, it would be contradictory for one to become more than the other after removing a finite amount. The conclusion is that the infinite series cannot be actual; it leads to a contradiction. This is a classic kalam argument for the finitude of the temporal series.

Key Terms
لا يتناهى
غير متناه
الزايد مثل الناقص
الماضي
الباقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite sets can be compared by size in the same way as finite sets.
Correction: Removing a finite amount from an actual infinite would not change its size, but the argument shows that if you transfer a finite portion, the past and future become unequal, contradicting their initial equality in infinity.

Checkpoint
What does the mutakallim conclude from the premise that both past and future are infinite?
A) The past is greater than the future.
B) The future is greater than the past.
C) The surplus would equal the deficient.
D) They are not equal.

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that an actual infinite temporal series leads to logical contradiction, so time must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the past is not infinite, that means your life has a definite starting point given by the Creator. Are you living each day as if it is a finite gift from Him, or do you waste time as if it were endless? What one change can you make today to treat time as precious?
?
The argument shows that an actual infinite leads to absurdity. Yet we often act as if our excuses or delays are infinite — 'I will repent later, I have time.' How does the finitude of time challenge you to stop postponing what matters most to your soul?
?
Juwayni points to a contradiction in the idea of an infinite past. Does your own heart sometimes assume that the world has always been here, reducing your sense of dependence on the Creator? How will you remind yourself daily that time began because Allah willed it?

8 min
Juwayni's Second Argument Against Infinite Individuals
Juwayni uses causal order and the coexistence of souls to argue against infinite individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's second argument using natural causal order and the problem of coexisting souls to prove a finite chain of individuals.

Core Idea
Infinite coexistent souls are impossible, so individuals cannot be infinite.

This passage presents a powerful argument from the order of nature and the problem of souls. Juwayni shows that even if individuals are successive, their souls are simultaneous, creating an impossibility if individuals were infinite. Let's examine his reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the distinction between conventional order (wadʿī) and natural order (ṭabīʿī). Juwayni argues that even if conventional order is absent, natural order exists.
Natural order refers to causal relations, like cause-effect or parent-child.
2
Understand the causal analogy
Juwayni compares effects depending on causes to children depending on parents. Just as causes must end in a first cause, parents must end in a first parent. The opponent claims infinite individuals, but this analogy suggests a finite chain.
The opponent (presumably atheists or materialists) denies a first parent.
3
Identify the problem of souls
The argument shifts to souls: each human has a rational soul that persists after death. If individuals are infinite, then there are infinitely many souls coexisting in the afterlife. This is impossible because an actual infinite of coexistent beings is rationally rejected.
This is a famous argument in kalam against infinite regress.
4
Consider the objection and reply
The opponent may claim souls have no order. Juwayni replies that if individuals are ordered by parent-child, so are souls, because the relation of parent to child imposes a similar order on their souls. Thus the order is not avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: أنه لو لم يتحقق في الحركات والأشخاص ترتيب وضعي فقد تحقق فيها ترتيب طبيعي؛ كالعلل والمعلولات فإنها لا بد أن تكون متناهية بالاتفاق، وذلك أن كل معلول فهو ممكن الوجود بذاته، وإنما يجب وجوده بعلته، فيتوقف وجوده على وجود علته، والكلام في علته كذلك حتى يتناهى إلى علة أولى ليست ممكنة لذاتها، كذلك نسبة كل والد إلى مولود نسبة العلة إلى معلولها، فيتوقف وجود الولد على وجود الوالد، وكذلك القول في الوالد، فهلا قلت: ينتهي إلى والد أول ومع ذلك فالأشخاص عندكم لا تتناهى، ثم ليعدد لكل شخص إنساني قد وجد نفس ناطقة وهي باقية مجتمعة في الوجود، فالأشخاص إذا كانت لا تتناهى وجاز وجودها لأنها متعاقبة لا مجتمعة معا في الوجود، فما قولك في النفوس فإنها مجتمعة وهي لا تتناهى؟ فإن قال: ليس لها ترتيب طبيعي ولا وضعي قيل: إذا ترتبت الأشخاص والدا ومولودا ترتبت النفوس أيضا ذلك الترتيب؛ لأن من العوارض واللوازم المعينة للنفوس كونها بحيث إن صدرت عن أشخاصها أشخاص فهذه النسبة باقية ببقائها، ولذلك النوع ترتيب.
The second aspect: that if there is not realized in motions and individuals a conventional order, there is realized in them a natural order; like causes and effects, for they must necessarily be finite by consensus. That is because every effect is possible in existence in itself, and it is only necessary by its cause, so its existence depends on its cause's existence, and the discussion about its cause similarly until it terminates at a first cause that is not possible in itself. Similarly, the relation of every parent to child is the relation of a cause to its effect, so the existence of the child depends on the existence of the parent, and likewise the statement about the parent. So why don't you say: it terminates at a first parent? Yet individuals according to you are infinite. Then let him count for each human individual a rational soul that has come into existence, and it is abiding and accumulated in existence. So if individuals are infinite and their existence is permissible because they are successive, not coexistent together in existence, what do you say about souls? For they are coexistent and they are infinite? If he says: they have neither a natural nor a conventional order, it is said: if individuals are ordered as parent and child, then souls also are ordered by that order; because among the accidents and necessary concomitants that specify souls is the way that if individuals originate from their individuals, this relation remains with their remaining, and therefore the type has order.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against infinite regress in individuals. It argues that even if there is no conventional (wadʿī) order among motions and individuals, there is a natural (ṭabīʿī) order, like the causal chain between causes and effects, which must be finite. The argument draws an analogy between causes and effects and the chain of parents and children. Just as causes terminate at a first cause, so too should parents terminate at a first parent. But the opponent claims that individuals (like humans) are infinite in number (they don't have a first father). The argument then raises a difficulty about souls: if individuals are infinite, their souls (nufūs) survive after death and are all coexistent in the world of spirits. So if the individuals are infinite, the souls would be infinite in number and coexistent, which is impossible because an actual infinite in coexistent beings is rejected. The opponent may object that souls do not have a natural or conventional order. The reply is that if individuals are ordered generationally (parent to child), then souls also have that order because their existence depends on the bodies they come from, and the relation of parent-child imposes a relational order on the souls. So the souls are not simply infinite in an unordered manner; even if the individuals are successive, the souls are simultaneous and require an order to avoid infinite coexistent beings. This reinforces the argument for a first individual (Adam) and thus a finite series.

Key Terms
ترتيب وضعي
ترتيب طبيعي
العلل والمعلولات
علة أولى
النفوس
متعاقبة
مجتمعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that if individuals are successive, an infinite number is possible because they do not all exist at once.
Correction: Juwayni shows that souls are not successive but coexistent, so infinite individuals would imply infinite coexistent souls, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the argument about souls in this passage?
A) Souls are finite because they are successive.
B) Souls are infinite but not coexistent.
C) If individuals are infinite, then souls would be infinite and coexistent, which is impossible.
D) Souls have no order.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress of individuals is impossible because it leads to an infinite number of coexisting souls, which is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Juwayni argues that every effect requires a cause, and the chain must end in a First Cause. If I trace back the chain of influences on my own life—my parents, teachers, circumstances—am I fully aware that they are all ultimately contingent on the First Cause? Do I live as if my existence depends on Allah every moment, or do I take it for granted?
?
The argument demands that finite beings cannot produce an infinite chain. If I reflect on my own mortality and the countless souls that have preceded me, am I truly living with the urgency that this finite life is moving toward an eternal coexistence? What would I change today if I truly believed I will meet every soul that ever lived?
?
Juwayni shows that souls are ordered because they relate to bodies parent-child. If my soul is tied to my physical lineage and actions, am I mindful that my deeds affect not only my soul but also the order of relationships? In what specific ways am I neglecting the responsibility of being a cause (positive or negative) for the souls around me?

8 min
Finitude of Motions via the Present Cycle
Juwayni proves finitude of past motions by analyzing the present cycle and the nature of time as counter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Juwayni uses the present cycle to prove that past events and motions are finite.

Core Idea
The present cycle is the key to proving that the past is finite.

Juwayni presents a second proof for the finitude of past events, focusing on the current cycle. By analyzing the nature of time as a counter and the fact that the past ends with the present, he argues that motions must be finite. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let Juwayni's argument flow naturally.
Notice the phrase 'الدورة التي نحن فيها' (the cycle we are in) – this is the crux.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage argues that past motions are finite. The key move is isolating the present moment and considering the past as ended with it.
Juwayni clarifies that finitude does not depend on future motions.
3
Follow the logical chain
From the present, each motion has a first and last; finitude from one end implies finitude from the other. Then, because motions are counted by time, time itself must be finite.
Consider the analogy: if the counted (motions) is finite, the counter (time) is also finite.
4
Understand the number argument
The passage ends by addressing a potential question: is the number of motions finite? Juwayni says any existing number is capable of increase and decrease, hence finite.
This reinforces the conclusion that the number of past motions is finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان آخر في أن ما لا يتناهى من الحوادث والحركات لا يتحقق في الوجود وذلك أنا نفرض الكلام في الدورة التي نحن فيها، ولا شك أن ما مضى قد انتهى بها، وما انتهى بشيء فقد تناهى. ومعنى قولنا: إن ما مضى قد انتهى أنا إذا أردنا قصر النظر على ما نحن فيه وقطعناه عما سيأتي اقتضى ذلك تناهي الحركات الماضية؛ إذ تناهي الحركات الماضية لم يكن مشروطا فيها لحوق الحركات الآتية، فما من حركة توجد وتعدم إلا وقد انقضت حركات قبلها بلا نهاية، فيوجد أبدا طرف منها متناهيا ويكون ذلك الطرف آخر الماضي وأولا لما بقي: فكل حركة أو كل دورة فقد تحقق لها أول وآخر، وإذا تناهى من طرف فقد تناهى من الطرف الثاني، فالحركات كلها إذا في ذواتها متناهية أولا وآخرا، وهي معدودة بالزمان والزمان هو العاد للحركات، فالمعدود إذا يتناهى أولا وآخرا كذلك يلزم أن يتناهى العاد أولا وآخرا. يبقى أن يقال: أهي متناهية عددًا أم لا؟ قلنا: وكل عدد موجود فهو قابل للزيادة والنقصان والأكثر والأقل فهو متناه، وكل فهو متناه، وكل عدد موجود فهو متناه.
Another proof that what does not terminate of events and motions does not come into existence. That is because we consider the cycle (dawra) we are in, and there is no doubt that what has passed has ended with it, and what ends with something is finite. And the meaning of our statement: 'that what has passed has ended' is that if we intend to restrict our view to what we are in and cut it off from what will come, that necessitates the finitude of past motions; since the finitude of past motions is not conditional on the attachment of future motions. So no motion exists and ceases except that motions before it have passed without end, so there always exists a finite extremity of them, and that extremity is the last of the past and the first of what remains: so every motion or every cycle has a first and a last. And if it is finite from one extremity, it is finite from the other extremity. So all motions are in their essences finite from first and last, and they are counted by time, and time is the counter of motions. So if the counted is finite from first and last, it follows that the counter is finite from first and last. It remains to be said: Are they finite in number or not? We said: And every existing number is capable of increase and decrease and more and less, so it is finite, and every finite [number], and every existing number is finite.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is another proof for the finitude of events and motions, particularly by analyzing the present cycle (the current time). The argument starts by noting that the past has ended with the present cycle. If something ends with something, it is finite. To clarify, if we isolate the present moment and ignore the future, the past motions must be finite because their finitude does not depend on future motions. The argument then states that for any motion that exists and ceases, there are motions before it without end, but there always exists a finite extremity of them, which is the last of the past and the first of what remains. So every motion or cycle has a first and a last. If it is finite from one extremity, it is finite from the other. Therefore, all motions are finite in their essences from first to last. They are counted by time, and time is the counter (ʿādd) of motions. If the counted (al-maʿdūd) is finite, the counter must also be finite. Finally, it addresses the question of finitude in number: any existing number is capable of increase and decrease, so it is finite. Hence, the number of motions is finite.

Key Terms
الدورة
تناهي
الزمان
المعدود
العدد
الزيادة والنقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes an infinite past that has a beginning.
Correction: The proof does not assume a first motion; it argues from the present cycle that the past ends here, implying finitude without assuming a starting point.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about time from the finitude of motions?
A) Time is infinite.
B) Time is the counter of motions, so it must be finite.
C) Time is not related to motions.
D) Time is circular.

Key Takeaway
Juwayni's proof shows that both motions and time are finite by analyzing the present cycle and the nature of counting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Juwayni argues that the past has ended with the present cycle. When you consider your own past actions, do you truly see this moment as the boundary where they end, or do you carry their weight as if they are still ongoing? What would it mean to accept that the past is truly finite and that this moment is the last of it?
?
If time itself is finite and counted by the limited number of motions, how does that change the way you value each passing moment? Are you living as if every moment is precious and finite, or do you act as if there is an infinite supply of time?
?
Juwayni says that any existing number is capable of increase and decrease, so it must be finite. Consider the number of your days. Do you live with the awareness that your days are countable and limited, or do you behave as if they are infinite? What would it take for you to truly internalize that your life is finite?

Refuting the Materialist Objection on Celestial Motions
Ash-Shahrastani refutes a materialist objection about infinite celestial motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the materialist argument about infinite motions of Saturn and the Moon, revealing a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Infinite motions of different spheres cannot be equated—Saturn's periphery exceeds the Moon's pole.

The materialists (Dahriyya) argued that the motions of Saturn and the Moon are both infinite, yet Saturn's motions are more numerous—a contradiction. They tried to resolve it by adjusting for distance. Ash-Shahrastani counters by highlighting the fundamental difference between peripheral and polar motions, showing the contradiction remains.

Guided Path
1
Understand the materialist objection
Read how the materialists claim that Saturn's motions are infinite and more numerous than the Moon's, leading to a contradiction.
Note the key point: both motions are infinite, but one is greater.
2
See the attempted resolution
The opponent tries to resolve the contradiction by accounting for the shorter distance of the Moon's sphere, making the motions equal in time.
This relies on the assumption that distance affects speed equivalently.
3
Grasp the author's refutation
The author points out that Saturn's sphere is the periphery and the Moon's is the pole, making their motions qualitatively different. Thus the contradiction remains.
The peripheral motion is inherently more voluminous than polar motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جملة الإلزامات على الدهرية أن حركات زُحل وهو في الفلك السابع مثل حركات القمر وهو في الفلك الأول؛ من حيث إن كل واحد من النوعين غير متناه، ومعلوم بالضرورة أن حركات زُحل أكثر من حركات القمر، فهي إذا مثلها وأكثر منها، وذلك من أمحل المحال وأبلغ في التناقض. فإن قيل: إن قصرت مسافة فلك القمر عن فلك زُحل، فقد طال زمان القمر بمقدار قصر المسافة حتى قطع القمر فلكه بمثل ما قطع زحل فلكه؛ فقد تساويا في الحركة. قلنا: وما قولكم في حركة فلك زُحل فإنها حركات المحيط، وحركات فلك القمر حركات القطب، فهما متساويان فيما لا يتناهى، وحركات فلك زُحل أضعاف حركات فلك القمر ولا جواب عنه.
Among the objections against the materialists (Dahriyya) is that the motions of Saturn, which is in the seventh sphere, are like the motions of the Moon, which is in the first sphere, in that each of the two types is infinite. It is known by necessity that the motions of Saturn are more numerous than the motions of the Moon. So they are both like it and more than it, and that is one of the most absurd impossibilities and the utmost in contradiction. If it is said: If the distance of the sphere of the Moon is shorter than the sphere of Saturn, then the time of the Moon has lengthened by the amount of the shortness of the distance, until the Moon traverses its sphere in the same way that Saturn traverses its sphere; so they become equal in motion. We reply: And what do you say about the motion of the sphere of Saturn? For it is the motions of the periphery, and the motions of the sphere of the Moon are the motions of the pole. They are equal with respect to what is infinite, and the motions of the sphere of Saturn are multiples of the motions of the sphere of the Moon, and there is no answer to it.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage refutes a materialist (Dahriyya) objection by examining the motions of Saturn and the Moon. The materialist claims that both motions are infinite, but Saturn's motions are more numerous than the Moon's, leading to a contradiction (the same infinite set being both equal and greater). The opponent then objects that if we adjust for the shorter distance of the Moon's sphere, the times equalize, making motions equal. The author counters by pointing out that Saturn's sphere is the outermost (periphery) while the Moon's is the innermost (pole); their motions are qualitatively different. Even if considered infinite, Saturn's motions are still multiples of the Moon's, preserving the contradiction. The author asserts this is unanswerable.

Key Terms
الدهرية
حركات زحل
حركات القمر
غير متناه
المحيط
القطب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that equalizing the times of motion across different distances resolves the paradox of infinite motions.
Correction: The author shows that the type of motion (peripheral vs polar) makes them incomparable even if rates are equal, so the contradiction remains.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that shortening the Moon's distance makes motions equal?
A) By denying the Moon's motion
B) By pointing out Saturn's motions are peripheral and the Moon's are polar, so they are not comparable in that way
C) By agreeing that both motions are finite
D) By claiming the materialists are ignorant

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that the materialists cannot equate Saturn's peripheral infinite motions with the Moon's polar infinite motions, preserving the contradiction.

This lesson shows how even materialists are forced into contradictions. Is your own conviction in the existence of Allah built on such rational evidence, or is it merely inherited? If you were challenged today, could you present this argument with clarity?

The opponent in the passage offered a plausible-sounding answer, but it crumbled under scrutiny. In your own spiritual life, where might you be accepting surface-level justifications that don't hold up to deeper reflection? What is one area of your faith or practice that you need to examine more critically this week?
?
The author asserts that the materialist objection has 'no answer.' Do you feel confident in the rational proofs for Allah's existence, or is there doubt lurking? This passage is a challenge: if you truly believe this argument is sound, it should move you to action. What will you do this week to strengthen your understanding and ability to articulate this proof?

The Threefold Classification of Duration (al-Mudda)
The author classifies duration into three possibilities and refutes infinite time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible meanings of 'duration' (al-mudda) and why infinite time cannot be actual.

Core Idea
Duration is either an existent, an estimate, or non-existence; only pure non-existence avoids the problem of infinitude.

In this passage, the author provides a second answer to an objection that presupposes duration must be either finite or infinite. He begins by asking what 'duration' really means, offering three distinct possibilities. By analyzing each option, he shows that the assumption of infinite past time is not a necessary consequence of any valid concept of duration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Pay attention to the three options he presents.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Find the three definitions of duration: actual existent, estimated existent (measure), and pure non-existence. Notice how the author examines each.
The passage starts with 'أهي عبارة عن أمر موجود أو عن تقدير موجود أم عن عدم بحت'.
3
Follow the refutation of infinite time
See how each option either cannot precede the world (first), or cannot be actualized as an infinite collective (second). The key is that mental estimation does not imply actual existence.
The author gives examples: another world, infinite numbers.
4
Grasp the conclusion about pure non-existence
If duration is pure non-existence, it has no finitude or infinitude. Thus the objection that duration must be either finite or infinite is resolved.
The final sentence: 'فلا يتناهى ولا لا يتناهى في عدم البحث'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب آخر عن التقسيم نقول : ما المعنى بالمدة ؛ أهي عبارة عن أمر موجود أو عن تقدير موجود أم عن عدم بحت ؟ فإن كان أمرًا موجودًا محققا فهو من العالم فلا يكون قبل العالم، وأيضا الموجود إما قايم بنفسه أو قايم بغيره، وعلى الوجهين جميعاً لا يمكن تقديره قبل العالم، وإن كان أمرًا مقدرًا وجوده، والتقديرات الوهمية تجویزات، وليس كلما يجوزه العقل أو يقدره الوهم يمكن وجوده جملة حاصلة، فإن وجود عالم آخر، وكذلك إلى ما لا يتناهى مما يجوز يقدره الوهم، وكذلك الأعداد التي لا تتناهى مما يقدره الوهم إلا مما يجوزه العقل. وبالجملة الجايزات كلها لا يمكن أن توجد جملة حاصلة، فيكون ما لا يتناهى مما يحصره الوجود وذاك محال، فعلم أن تقدير أزمنة غير متناهية لا يكون كالتحقيق، وما يمكن وجوده جملة معًا أو متعاقباً لا يمكن أن يكون مشحونًا بما لا يتناهى، وإن كانت المدة عبارة عن عدم بحت فلا يتناهى ولا لا يتناهى في عدم البحث.
And another answer regarding the division: We say: What is meant by duration? Is it an expression for an existent thing, or for a measure of an existent, or for pure non-existence? If it is an actually existent thing, then it is part of the world, so it cannot be before the world. Also, the existent is either self-subsistent or subsistent in another. In both cases, it cannot be estimated before the world. And if it is an estimated existence, and imaginative estimations are conjectures, and not everything that the intellect allows or the imagination estimates can actually exist as a collective whole, because the existence of another world, and likewise to infinity of what the intellect allows and the imagination estimates, and likewise the infinite numbers that the imagination estimates except what the intellect allows. In summary, all possibilities cannot exist as a collective whole. So what is infinite of what existence encompasses is impossible. Thus it is known that the estimation of infinite times is not like verification. And what can exist collectively or successively cannot be filled with what is infinite. And if duration is an expression for pure non-existence, then there is no finitude nor infinitude in pure non-existence.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a second answer (jawab akhar) to an objection concerning the classification of duration (al-mudda). The author begins by asking what 'duration' means, proposing three possibilities: (1) an actual existent, (2) a measure of an existent, or (3) pure non-existence. He then examines each possibility. If duration is an actual existent, it would be part of the world and thus cannot be prior to the world. If it is a measure of an existent, it also cannot be estimated before the world because any existent is either self-subsistent or dependent, and in both cases it cannot be prior. If duration is estimated existence (i.e., merely imagined), he argues that imaginative estimations are mere conjectures; not everything the intellect allows or imagination estimates can actually exist as a collective whole. For example, the existence of another world or infinite numbers are things the mind can conceive, but they cannot all exist simultaneously. Therefore, an infinite collection of possibilities cannot be actualized. Consequently, the supposition of infinite times is not a real possibility. Finally, if duration is pure non-existence, then it has neither finitude nor infinitude. Thus, the objection that duration must be either finite or infinite is resolved.

Key Terms
المدة
تقدير
عدم
جايزات
لا يتناهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mental estimation of infinite time implies actual infinite time.
Correction: The author clarifies that mental possibility does not entail actual existence; an infinite collective whole cannot be actual.

Checkpoint
According to the author, if duration is pure non-existence, what can be said about its finitude?
A) It must be finite.
B) It must be infinite.
C) It is neither finite nor infinite.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Duration as pure non-existence avoids the problem of infinite time, as it has no finitude or infinitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely assume that your future is endless. But this lesson shows that imagined infinite time does not correspond to reality. When you delay an act of worship or a repentance, are you building your life on a mental estimation that may never exist? What will you change today because time is finite and not infinite?
?
The author argues that pure non-existence has no finitude or infinitude. Before the world, there was only non-existence. Does this awe you that Allah alone is eternal, without any duration accompanying Him? How does this truth press you to take your present moment seriously for your Hereafter?
?
You mentally estimate many possibilities—what you will do tomorrow, next year, or later in life. But not everything estimated can exist collectively. Which 'future possibility' are you trusting in that may never materialize? How can you stop postponing your duty to Allah based on an imagined future?
